FOREWORD
Every day, planners, designers and transport authorities are faced with the challenge of 
making public transport spaces as inclusive as possible and creating systems that can be 
used by all passengers while still operating efficiently. Modern and pioneering projects 
in particular, which are incorporating an increasing number of sensors and technologies, 
are raising new issues in the areas of passenger guidance, accessibility and integration. 
The planned introduction of an autonomous system on the Copenhagen S-Train 
therefore requires a solution that meets both the technical and personal requirements 
of passengers. This results in an interesting interaction between functional efficiency 
and personal positive passenger experience.
The opportunity to write my Master‘s thesis in cooperation with DSB and to be involved 
in such a relevant and future-oriented project has excited and motivated me from the 
very beginning, as it closely matches my personal interests, competences and beliefs. 
Applying the skills I have learnt during my studies to such a relevant project, which is used 
by hundreds of thousands of people every day, this work provides a unique environment 
to put theoretical knowledge into practice and create better design solutions.
I would therefore like to thank Imke Wies Mulder-Van Mil at DSB for giving me this 
opportunity and for actively supporting me throughout the project with integration into 
the company, data and other assistance. These resources, insight into the company‘s 
work and personal recommendation were of great value to my work. This has all contributed 
to a project that has not only produced interesting research, results and solutions, but 
can also serve the company for inclusive design in the future. 
Special thanks are due to my supervisors at DTU, whose expert guidance and valuable 
feedback over the past six months has given me direction and structure through the 
complex project. Josef Peter Helmut Oehmen has clearly helped to shape the direction of 
the project through regular meetings and relevant feedback. The practical and scientific 
advice and honest input has been crucial in developing a relevant and realistic solution. 
Marie Louise Pollmann-Larsen from Skylab also made this work relevant, being supportive 
and encouraging. The collaboration provided me with a large pool of resources that I 
could use in a relevant way to work on the project. Whether it was the opportunity to 
connect with people and organisations in relevant fields, or to use Skylabs‘ resources 
for my work. This allowed me to physically present and test many of the approaches 
developed, especially in the solutions part of the thesis.
Last but not least, I would like to thank all the people who participated in the project through 
interviews, passenger accompaniment or workshop participation, and whose knowledge 
and experience clearly contributed to the success of the project. Their commitment, 
knowledge and openness helped the project in many cases to obtain relevant and often 
overlooked information that contributed immensely to the development of realistic 
solutions.
This work combines research, design and subsequent practical application with the aim 
of making public transport more accessible, understandable and inclusive for everyone. 
I hope that the research conducted, the approaches developed and the workshop can 
provide valuable input for the future design of public transport by DSB, the DTU or perhaps 
even other institutions.
Many thanks to everyone who has accompanied and supported me on this journey.

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
This six-month master‘s thesis looks at the design of stations in the context of the 
automation of the Copenhagen S-Bahn over the next ten years, with a focus on user-
centred redesign. The planned introduction of driverless trains will create many 
opportunities for DSB, particularly in the areas of efficiency, operations and punctuality. 
However, the loss of personal interaction between staff and passengers also poses 
challenges in terms of usability, orientation and accessibility.
The project aims to improve the passenger experience by analysing the interaction 
between passengers and the station and its various elements, and developing 
innovative design solutions. To achieve this, a structured design process has been 
applied in association with DTU, which runs through the design process from research 
and problem analysis to practical application. The central aspect of the solution is the 
development of a workshop tool that can support DSB‘s future infrastructure projects 
to address the problems and needs of passengers, especially those with disabilities. 
 
During the course of the project it became clear that specific solutions to individual 
problems in public transport are often difficult to implement because they require specific 
adaptations that vary from station to station and from passenger to passenger. In order 
to recognise these differences, the approach of conducting interviews, observations or 
accompanying passengers was chosen to gather relevant information about everyday life 
in the suburban railway system. This approach highlighted the need for a generic solution 
that would highlight the difficulties faced by passengers and create empathy among 
decision makers, designers and engineers to ensure that the problems are addressed. 
 
The results of this work provide DSB with valuable insights and a versatile tool to make 
future projects more intuitive and inclusive. The current focus is on the autonomous 
suburban railway project, but its flexible structure will allow it to be integrated into the 
company‘s planning processes and culture in the long term.

1
INTRODUCTION
User-centred design plays an important role in how people interact with their environment in public spaces. For public transport, 
this is particularly relevant. Passengers often associate the use of public transport not only with the actual aspect of travelling, 
but also with a variety of emotions. As a result, public transport can significantly shape the image that people hold of a city. In this 
phase of the project, the foundations are laid and the purpose of the work is defined. This goal has been worked out and defined 
in collaboration with all stakeholders involved in the project. This phase serves as the foundation and starting point for the design 
process and the development of a master‘s thesis.
NÆSTE STOP
005

==PAGE:12==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS

011
1.1 MOTIVATION
This Master‘s thesis uses the transition to an autonomous S-train system as an 
opportunity to investigate station design and passenger behaviour from a user-
centred perspective. While DSB‘s primary focus is on implementing the technical 
and regulatory framework, the organisation recognises that certain aspects of the 
passenger experience may be overlooked due to budget and resource constraints. 
Therefore, this project aims to provide relevant research, insights and possible 
solutions to address these gaps and ensure a well-functioning, inclusive and user-
friendly station experience. 
The aim of this Master‘s thesis is to investigate how a holistic redesign of 
Copenhagen‘s S-Train stations could affect DSB‘s project to fully automate the 
S-Train over the next ten years. The aim is to explore the area and validate possible 
solutions to create a better passenger experience for S-train passengers. While 
regulations and specifications for technical solutions and accessibility are already 
being implemented by DSB in existing stations, there is a wide range of solutions and 
options that can affect the passenger experience for passengers with and without 
disabilities. In this area, the Master‘s thesis will explore possible solutions in order 
to develop an end product that will assist the DSB project. In particular, the focus is 
on human-centred design with an emphasis on science and technology, leaving no 
one behind from the university. It is important to analyse the needs and challenges 
of the users of the system and to develop solutions that can help these passengers. 
 
The aim of trains and stations is to serve the widest possible range of passengers. 
However, this inevitably causes problems as the whole infrastructure around them 
needs to be designed to be as inclusive as possible. Because people and passengers 
and their needs are so diverse, and because a fully inclusive infrastructure is 
expensive, this often leads to problems that exclude people and passengers who 
lack certain abilities and opportunities. This not only denies them the opportunity 
to use public transport, but can often have a far-reaching impact on their social 
life, especially if they are dependent on public transport due to a disability. In a 
business context, people with disabilities are often seen as a small part of the 
overall target group. However, addressing this target group in a targeted way often 
requires major changes that require significant investment. As a result, inclusive 
and accessible solutions are often only possible through regulatory intervention 
by public authorities. However, especially in the field of public transport, it is 
necessary to address users and individuals as directly as possible. Furthermore, 
solutions for specific passengers in difficult cases are often also helpful for the 
general public, who then benefit from the introduction of targeted solutions. 
 
DSB‘s plan to fully automate the entire Copenhagen S-Train network with 
driverless trains within the next ten years brings many benefits for passengers, 
but also some challenges, especially for people with disabilities. For this reason, it 
makes sense to tackle this task together with DSB and DTU in this master project 
and to explore the area of an inclusive S-Train station. This fits in well with the 
general aims of the Design and Innovation programme, where many projects 
focus on developing products, solutions and systems for a wide range of users. 
 
 On a personal level, this combines many of my own interests, which have been present 
since my childhood for family reasons in infrastructure, and in particular railways. 
During my bachelor‘s degree in product design, it was always very important to 
focus on the importance and role of the user at the centre of projects. This creates 
a coherent overall picture for a feasible and meaningful project in the field of design 
and innovation and public transport. Aimed at finding and solving some relevant 
problems in this field, benefiting DSB and DTU by providing research and solutions.

1.2 COMMISSION
Currently, DSB does not have a dedicated team focused on how passengers interact 
with the station environment in the transition to a fully autonomous network. 
This area is often considered a soft factor, despite its significant impact on the 
functionality, accessibility and overall success of the new system. For passengers 
with disabilities in particular, an intuitive, accessible and predictable station 
environment is essential. The transition to driverless trains will fundamentally change 
the way passengers navigate stations, access information and seek assistance. 
 
The project will focus on elements that influence passenger behaviour and the overall 
travel experience, particularly in the context of an autonomous rail system. DSB hopes 
to identify key challenges, propose potential improvements and explore innovative 
station design concepts that support seamless navigation, accessibility and usability. 
Through this collaboration, DSB seeks a fresh external perspective to uncover both 
known and previously unidentified challenges related to station-user interaction. 
 
In order to fulfil the academic requirements of the DTU Master‘s programme in Design 
and Innovation, this thesis will be structured according to the predefined research 
framework. Special emphasis will be placed on close collaboration with supervisors at 
DTU and relevant stakeholders at DSB to ensure a meaningful and applicable outcome 
that meets both academic expectations and real-world implementation possibilities. 
 
Overall, this is a unique opportunity to bridge the gap between technical innovation 
and human-centred station design, ensuring that Copenhagen‘s future autonomous 
S-Train system is not only operationally efficient, but also intuitive and accessible 
for all passengers.
Imke Wies Mulder-Van Mil
Passenger Experience Design Lead, DSB
Responsible for the future S-Train programme as Passenger 
Experience Designer, working across all areas of the project, 
including technical regulations and specifications, station 
design and the rolling stock team.

integration of this principle in many projects. Through the Leave 
No One Behind initiative, a large pool of contacts and knowledge 
for inclusive design is also available.

1.3 PROBLEM STATMENT
A well-functioning public transport system is vital for a modern city. It is an essential 
part of the way people travel, commute and engage in leisure activities. Especially 
considering the future, it is a big step towards carbon neutrality as an environmentally 
friendly alternative to other means of transport. In Copenhagen, the S-Train 
network, with its distinctive red trains, 87 stations and a daily passenger volume of 
around 350,000, is the most important part of the city‘s public transport network. 
 
90 years after the opening of the first S-Train line in Copenhagen in 1934, the 
system is now facing a fundamental change. On the approach of the system‘s 
100th birthday in ten years‘ time, DSB, the operator of the S-Train network, is 
planning to operate the entire network completely autonomously. This makes the 
project one of the largest infrastructure projects in Denmark and one of a kind in 
Europe. However, such a project also brings with it many challenges, particularly 
in the area of technical safety systems, as well as changes for passengers. 
The transition to autonomous trains will eliminate the interaction between passengers 
and drivers. However, as this is currently still a regular means for passengers to 
obtain information, the loss of this could lead to problems. Therefore, it is necessary 
to develop alternative solutions for passenger information in order to maintain a 
pleasant and inclusive travel experience. In the DSB project, the company is putting a 
lot of thought and investment into technical solutions and systems. Something that 
is often somewhat overlooked are the challenges and problems that passengers, 
especially those with disabilities, experience when using the system. The move 
to autonomous trains can be used as an opportunity to address these issues and 
ultimately create not only a more efficient but also a more inclusive urban transport 
system.
1.4 GOAL
This Master‘s thesis focuses on the development of a system, concept or product that improves the passenger experience at the S-Train station. The developed result aims 
to improve the passenger experience in Copenhagen‘s future S-Train system. While automation offers operational benefits, it also brings challenges in terms of navigation, 
orientation and accessibility. These challenges need to be analysed and understood in order to create meaningful solutions and an overall better experience for every passenger.

of the project.

2.1 DTU
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet is Denmark‘s leading university for engineering and 
technical sciences. Founded in 1829 by Hans Christian Ørsted, the university is now 
home to more than 13,000 students from Denmark and around the world.
“DTU is recognized internationally as a leading university in the areas of the technical 
and the natural sciences, renowned for our business-oriented approach, our focus 
on sustainability, and our amazing study environment.“ [1] DTU offers a wide range 
of expertise in technical fields at all levels from Bachelor, Master to PhD. 
The University‘s programme covers a wide range of technical and natural sciences, 
including engineering, computing, science, environmental technology, biotechnology, 
chemistry and more. In all of these fields, the University emphasises the interdisciplinary 
approach to learning and combining theoretical knowledge with practical applications. 
The university‘s commitment to integrating industry cooperation into courses within 
the curriculum offers benefits both to students, who benefit from studying in a realistic 
environment, and to companies, who work together with academically-based research 
for innovative and future-proof results. In particular, DTU‘s Master‘s programmes offered 
exclusively in English, create an attractive environment for international students from 
all over the world. 
DTU‘s main campus in Lyngby, north of Copenhagen, offers a wide range of state-of-the-
art facilities. Modern classrooms, laboratories, libraries and workshops give students the 
opportunity to work on their projects in an ideal environment. The campus also offers 
many leisure activities, such as a sports facilities, bars and green areas. The same applies 
to the Ballerup campus.
FIGURE 4: DTU Auditorium

2.2 DESIGN AND INNOVATION
The programme Design and Innovation at DTU is offered at both bachelor‘s and master‘s 
level. The bachelor‘s level is targeted at Danish students and the master‘s level is 
offered exclusively in English offering international and Danish students to participate. 
Programmes at DTU are described as „research-based technological programmes at 
elite level“ [2] and offer deep knowledge in the respective field with a focus on industry 
and business-related study methods.
DTU‘s master programme, Design and Innovation, is based on the idea that science and art 
come together to create “innovative solutions that satisfy our economic, environmental 
and societal goals. The technical and organizational designs we develop will literally 
shape our future.“ [3] The program combines both technical disciplines and design 
science with the user based approach. Therefore people and society are always in the 
focus when approaching Complex problems and challenges in a structured manner.
Students on the study programme will work on projects to develop products, services 
and systems in the field of innovation. These projects reflect modern work environments 
and design approaches. There is a strong focus on working with companies, providing 
real-life scenarios and working in teams made up of people with different skills to create 
solutions and results that are both innovative and realistic. Through this approach the 
study program “aims to develop creative skills and abilities to analyse the interaction 
between the user, technology, organization and environment.“[3] The programme 
is designed to equip students with the essential skills to communicate problems and 
the following solution concepts, implement innovation processes in organisations and 
companys, and gain deep knowledge in technical fields.
A key element of the approach is the emphasis placed on collaborative work in 
multidisciplinary teams throughout projects. This approach fosters collaboration 
between various DTU departments, including civil and mechanical engineering and 
DTU entrepreneurship. By adopting this strategy, the study line draws expertise from 
other areas, contributing to a realistic and innovative approach to problem-solving. The 
opportunity for students to choose one semester of elective courses from all the fields 
offered at DTU also reflects this commitment to interdisciplinary learning.
By integrating design concepts and methodologies such as design thinking, co-creation 
or agile design, students are equipped with modern approaches for diverse career paths. 
Many students pursue careers in product development, design consultancy, innovation 
management or entrepreneurship. The realistic approach allows students to easily apply 
their acquired knowledge to real-life projects, contributing to a forward-thinking and 
innovative work culture.
The public transport sector fits well with the general approach of the study line, with 
its focus on sustainability in a social and environmental sense, and the design of user-
centred solutions with a strong emphasis on accessibility. Public transport plays a major 
role in addressing current challenges and offers many points of intervention where DTU´s 
design and innovation approach can be helpful.

2.3 DSB
Danske Statsbaner, or DSB for short, is Denmark‘s leading provider of passenger rail 
services. Throughout the company‘s long history, dating back to its foundation in 1885, 
it has seen many different faces of public transport by rail, from steam locomotives to 
modern high-speed intercity and regional trains. The company has evolved into the 
modern and dynamic business it is today and acts as the backbone for nationwide rail 
transport in Denmark, serving over 195 million passengers every year. 
DSB was established in 1885 to unify and centrally manage the various independent and 
individually operated railway lines in Denmark. In the early years, DSB focused mainly on 
expanding the national railway network to connect major cities and areas. Over the years, 
the company has made technological advances, upgrading the trains and signalling to 
provide modern and comfortable public transport, as the government recognises the 
need and importance of public transport to the economy and infrastructure.
As a state-owned company under the supervision of the Danish Ministry of Transport, 
the main objective is to provide efficient, punctual and customer-oriented train services 
throughout the country. Therefore, DSB operates an extensive network of regional, 
intercity, and suburban rail services, connecting the major cities of Denmark, but also 
other countries. In the country‘s capital, Copenhagen, the company also operates the 
urban S-train system.
With around 6000 employees of more than 70 nationalities [4] ,DSB offers a modern 
and diverse working environment where people from all walks of experience work to 
create an effective and usable public transport system. With Denmark playing a leading 
role in sustainability worldwide, DSB also aims to provide individuals with a reliable and 
environmentally friendly means of transport throughout the country. Major projects 
such as the full electrification of trains and the full automation of the S-train system 
by 2035 are important parts of DSB‘s vision for the future. While these major projects 
require a lot of resources and provide a glimpse into the company‘s future, DSB‘s day-
to-day activities range from operating and maintaining regular train services to planning 
station and train overhauls and procuring new rolling stock.
Operating within the public transportation sector necessitates collaboration among 
numerous stakeholders. DSB collaborates closely with the state-owned Banedanmark, 
which operates the majority of the tracks and a significant number of stations throughout 
the country. Collaboration with cities, communes, and other companies providing 
services and trains to DSB is fundamental. In urban areas, it is also of great importance 
to create quick and easy ways to transfer between different public transport methods, 
such as the Copenhagen Metro, trams and buses.

2.4 SERVICES
DSB offers a variety of services with different purposes, frequencies and passenger 
experience. These range from more frequent urban services for commuters and leisure 
travellers, to less frequent services between the country‘s major regional areas, to 
regular highspeed intercity services between the country‘s major cities, and trains that 
connect Denmark to Sweden and Germany. Each of these services plays a different role 
in the Danish transport system, therefore each one has different requirements, ways of 
operating and different target groups.
INTERNATIONAL TRAINS
In collaboration with other companies and public transport operators, DSB also offers 
a few  international routes. The most notable connections are to Sweden and the city 
of Malmö over the Öresund Bridge, which depart from Copenhagen [5].Another route 
departing from Copenhagen includes a direct service to the German city Hamburg [6].
These two options are quite different in terms of how they operate. The route to Sweden 
acts more like an urban regional train, connecting the greater areas of Copenhagen and 
Malmö with many stops and frequent trains, while the Hamburg connection is a fast 
intercity train with only a handful of stops.
INTERCITY TRAINS
DSB‘s intercity network offers fast travel between Denmark‘s major cities such as 
Copenhagen, Odense, Aarhus and Aalborg. With only a few stops at major stations and a 
high travel speed, these trains are designed to be more comfortable for longer journeys 
and offer a high level of passenger experince. The aim is to provide the country with a 
comfortable and fast connection between the largest cities, and to offer an alternative 
to the car and short flights.
REGIONAL TRAINS
Regional trains operated by DSB serve to connect cities, towns and larger population 
areas. These medium-distance services link regional centres with the capital and other 
key locations throughout the country by providing regular trains designed for commuting 
and leisure activities.
COPENHAGEN S-TRAIN
The S-Train system of Copenhagen provides a regular and efficient train service for 
the capital region of Denmark. With its frequent and fast train services, it provides a 
link between the city and the suburbs in the greater regional area for commuters and 
residents of the capital. With its dedicated network of tracks, stations, and trains, the 
S-Train system is tailored to meet the demands of frequent short-distance travel.
FIGURE 6: DSB IC3 Train
018
==PAGE:20==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
019
2.5 COPENHAGENS GOAL 2025
As a country, Denmark is known for having a very sustainable and environmentally friendly 
way of life. This is often used as a selling point in marketing and related fields to promote 
its leading role. Over the decades, the country has moved more and more towards green 
energy and overall sustainable consumption. This has enabled the country to “rank first 
in Environmental Performance Index 2022.“ [7] 
A major change in how Denmark runs as a country was caused by the 1973 oil crisis. At this 
point “about 90% of national energy supply was through imported oil.“[7] This reliance 
on imported oil created economic and social challenges throughout the country, leading 
to a decision to change its energy policy to focus on green and sustainable energy. The 
country‘s geographical location and large coastline favoured wind energy. Through 
political incentives and cooperation between public institutions and private companies, 
Denmark has developed a great amount of expertise in this field. This can be seen 
today in particular in the companies based in the country and the strong focus in the 
academic field, such as the study programme “Master of Science (MSc) in Sustainable 
Energy Systems“ [8] at DTU. As a result, Denmark has become a lucrative location for 
businesses from across all sectors and sees itself as a future-proof location by focussing 
on economically and environmentally sustainable growth.
As the social and economic centre of the country, the capital Copenhagen plays a vital 
role in this process. The city itself has even more concrete ambitions when it comes to a 
sustainable future, the goal is to become "carbon neutral by 2025“. [9] Often considered 
one of the most liveable cities in the world, Copenhagen blends a long history, great culture 
and a high standard of living. Combining both impressive old architecture with modern 
waterfront construction projects. The city is also worth living in thanks to its abundance 
of water. Canals, lakes and the proximity to the sea provide many idyllic and recreational 
areas, but also a growing threat from climate change “with increased rainfall, warmer 
weather and rising sea levels.“ [10] To address this challenge, the city has launched the 
2025 and 2035 Climate Plan. The 2025 plan is for the city to be completely CO2 neutral 
by that year. With an expected 20% increase in population, it is very important to focus 
on sustainable development and green infrastructure. Other points include a focus 
on energy efficiency in buildings and industry, and a strong emphasis on renewable 
energy. The city is committed to achieving this through public and private projects and 
government-led policies. In 2021, the city has “reduced CO2 emissions by 72.6% compared 
to the base year of the Climate Plan, 2005.“ [11] The 2035 plan aims to make the city even 
more sustainable and ultimately achieve net negative CO2 consumption for all residents.
FIGURE 7: Offshore Windturbines 
019
==PAGE:21==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
020
2.6 COPENHAGEN PUBLIC TRANSPORT
According to the climate plan for 2025, the transport sector plays a particularly important role in making the city CO2 neutral. Many of the city‘s residents have already switched to electric 
cars, which is made possible by the well-developed network of charging stations and infrastructure. [12] Even more important for the city, however, is a functioning and reliable public 
transport system to get residents and visitors from individual means of transport to sustainable forms of transport. The city already has a large public transport network based on various 
methods.
BUSSES
Although the metro has become the main mode of transport in the city centre, Copenhagen 
also relies on its extensive bus network.  Many lines use modern buses, often with electric 
propulsion and digital displays for real-time updates, providing more flexible connections 
for travellers. Special express lines and night buses provide additional connectivity and 
transport throughout all hours of the day.
FERRY
Operated as part of the public transport system, the city‘s harbour ferries offer a unique 
way of moving about Copenhagen. They connect key locations along the harbour, 
including Nyhavn, Refshaleøen, and the Opera House they offer a quick way of transport 
across the water. As well as being a convenient means of transport, they are also popular 
with tourists as they provide a scenic view of the city‘s waterfront. [13]
S-TRAIN
The Copenhagen S-Train is the backbone of the city‘s public transport network. The main 
focus is to provide train services for the greater Copenhagen area, connecting the city 
centre with the suburbs and surrounding areas. Frequent and reliable services on seven 
lines make it an important mode of transport for daily commuters and residents.
METRO
With its first line opened in 2000, the Copenhagen Metro is a modern example of urban 
transport. Fully automated and driverless trains run 24 hours a day, providing fast and 
frequent connections throughout the city centre. Of particular note is the City Ring, 
which provides a fast option for travelling in the city centre. The metro also connects to 
Copenhagen Airport. It also has been a major boost to the development of neighbourhoods 
such as Nordhavn and Ørestad.
Although not a direct part of the public transport system, there is another mode of transport that is crucial to the city of Copenhagen. Often referred to as the city of cyclists, Copenhagen 
has an extensive network of dedicated cycle lanes, bridges and bike-sharing schemes. [14]  Around 50 % of the city‘s residents commute by bike every day, making it an important part of 
urban life and culture. The city is therefore committed to creating an accessible and safe environment for cycling. Prioritising cycling in urban planning and other infrastructure projects 
is crucial to improving and maintaining this cycling culture in the city. Copenhagen‘s transport network reflects the city‘s identity as a forward-looking city. The combination of different 
modes of transport will play a crucial role as the city continues to grow and develop its commitment to sustainability and modern urban life. 
==PAGE:22==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
021
2.7 S-TRAIN
With the opening of the first line in 1934, Copenhagen‘s  S-train system has played an 
important role in the daily life and development of the Danish capital and has grown 
with the city. 
 
Inspired by similar systems in Germany, the aim of the S-train system was to provide an 
efficient suburban rail services for the city. Today, the network spans over 170 km, with 
86 stations on seven lines. The network is laid out in the distinctive five-finger design, 
connecting the city centre with several urban areas and suburbs to the north, east and 
south of the capital. [15] Only Line F runs differently, forming a semicircle around the 
east of the city centre and connecting with all the other lines, allowing connections 
in all directions. The current system serves over 300,000 passengers a day. Even with 
trains running every 10 minutes and further trains at peak times, the system is currently 
close to its maximum capacity. (Appendix 7.1.2)
A key feature of the Copenhagen S-Train is that passengers can take their bicycles 
on board free of charge. With spacious bike compartments in the current rolling 
stock, many passengers have adopted a way of travelling that relies on riding the bike 
to the station, taking the S-Train with the bike, and then cycling to their destination 
from the nearest S-Train stop. This is in the spirit of Copenhagen‘s cycling culture. 
 
DSB is currently in the early stages of a project to fully automate the entire S-Train system 
and switch to driverless trains. This change will allow higher frequency and service on all 
lines. This is also the focus of this master project to improve Copenhagen‘s suburban 
railway system by redesigning stations in relation to automation of the trains, with a 
particular focus on passenger accessibility.

3.1 DESIGN CONTEXT TRAINS
In the context of complex projects, design is often seen as simply one of the pieces 
defining aesthetics. However, design has a much deeper role to play in the modern world. 
Good design is the basis for functional and effective spaces. In the context of railways, 
design plays a key role in every aspect. 
The first thing that comes to mind when people think of design in the context of trains is 
the livery of the carriages, the seat patterns or the historic station buildings. But there 
is much more to these designs and products than just visual aesthetics. Train carriages, 
for example, should be designed from the inside out to provide the most comfortable and 
ergonomic seats possible, to minimise noise pollution on the train with partitions, and to 
make the positioning of luggage racks for boarding as efficient as possible. Intelligent 
design must also be used to create controlled and effective management of crowds 
at stations. A consistent and understandable design language for displays, maps and 
signage also plays a key role. (Appendix 7.1.2) When all this comes together, it creates 
an overall visual identity for a railway company. In this way, an aesthetic can be created 
that will be recognised from far away over generations. The ICE in Germany with its 
distinctive red ribbon [16], the shiny silver subway in New York or the extra-wide S-Train 
in Copenhagen, all these are examples of iconic railway design and serve as icons of city 
and country. They all contribute to recognition and, if used correctly, can lead to strong 
brand identity. To create a future-proof and user-friendly train infrastructure, many 
designers from different fields, such as grafic designers, product designers, architects, 
crowd management engineers, come together to create the best possible passenger 
experience and the most efficient train system.
There are three drivers of design and innovation in the railway sector. The main driving 
force in most countries is legislation, regulations and guidelines, due to the national 
importance of public transport and developed railway infrastructure. These design 
parameters, often set by higher 
authorities, must be taken 
into account when designing 
new projects or refurbishing 
existing infrastructure. Close 
cooperation 
between 
the 
various parties is essential 
to ensure efficient work and 
ultimately 
a 
satisfactory 
outcome. The second driving 
force is the data collected 
by the railway company. This 
describes 
customer 
needs 
and requirements in relation to 
market conditions. In this area, 
it is a question of integrating 
appropriate 
solutions 
and 
design results into the system 
for the target group and the 
time context. This is particularly important in order to meet the needs of the target 
group and thus maintain market share and exploit new potential. The third and final 
design factor is the innovation that the railway undertakes based on its own creativity 
and ideas. Through research and innovation, potential improvements and changes can 
be identified and implemented. The result can be pioneering solutions that can make a 
lasting difference to public transport.
Entirety of all decisions and activities

3.2 AUTOMATED TRAINS
There is a shift in the way rapid transit systems operate, with many new projects and 
existing metro lines being equipped with driverless trains and running autonomously.
The world‘s first fully automated, driverless rail system was designed and built in 
Copenhagen. All four lines are operated with autonomous trains based at a central control 
point. Its first line opened in 2002. [17] Copenhagen has very hard soil underneath the 
city, which makes it very expensive to dig out stations.This meant that the stations had to 
be smaller than in other metro systems. To compensate for this, higher train frequencies 
were needed to meet the passenger throughput targets. (Appendix 7.1.4)
This is where automation comes in with driverless trains. It is possible to achieve a high 
frequency service that results in sufficient passenger throughput. The Copenhagen 
metro is renowned for its service, with waiting times between trains of less than two 
minutes at peak times. In addition to increased efficiency, there are a number of other 
benefits of a driverless system. Through the automation it is possible to save costs by 
reducing the number of personnel neededed on board. This means that staff can be 
reassigned to more specific tasks such as ticket control, maintenance or operations. 
Another benefit of a driverless system is an improved passenger experience. The 
Copenhagen Metro, for example, offers very short intervals between trains, which is 
beneficial to the passenger experience. These systems also allow for longer operating 
hours, as there is no need for personnel to be driving the trains. Also beneficial for the 
passenger experience is that the movement of the train is very predictable, acceleration, 
speed and braking is the same at every station which provides a smoother ride for all 
passengers on board. Because of all these advantages, many existing metro systems 
are opting for an automated system in newly designed systems. In Europe, for example, 
line 14 now in Paris is fully automated. The city also has plans to upgrade existing lines 
with the driverless system. Singapore, known for its strict operating regulations and high 
public expectations for reliable infrastructure, has a number of automated metro lines in 
one of the most densely populated areas in the world. Hamburg is currently developing 
an autonomous metro line in the city. [18]
With all these advantages, there are also a number of challenges and considerations that 
need to be taken into account with the driverless train system. The initial investment in 
technology, safety systems and trains is very high. The aim is to make the investment pay 
off in the long term through cost savings and increased efficiency. These cost savings 
often come from savings in personnel, which is a balancing act when it comes to public 
acceptance. Job losses may be a public concern. 
FIGURE 11: Driverless train of the Copenhagen Metro
==PAGE:27==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
026
3.3 WHY CHANGE THE S-TRAIN?
DSB has set itself the goal of operating Copenhagen‘s S-Train system fully automatically 
by 2035. This will make it the first full S-Train system in the world to switch to driverless 
train technology. This decision is driven by a number of key factors, including improved 
efficiency and capacity, economic benefits and increased passenger satisfaction. 
The biggest advantage for the Copenhagen S-train will be the increased frequency of 
trains on all lines. This is especially important during peak hours. The system is currently 
operating close to maximum capacity. Shorter intervals between trains will allow more 
services and therefore more passengers. (Appendix 7.1.2) This can be achieved by relying 
on technological systems, as the operation is not dependent on human input. The 
aim is therefore also to improve punctuality and minimise delays. Running trains more 
frequently on existing lines will also increase the efficiency of the system. The existing 
track infrastructure only needs to be upgraded to accommodate driverless trains, giving 
DSB a more cost-effective way of increasing the capacity and efficiency of the S-Train 
without having to invest heavily in the construction of new tracks. This is aligned with 
the growing population of the Danish capital and supports the urban development. 
Passengers will benefit from these changes in several ways: increased capacity and 
frequency will allow commuters to travel more flexibly and less stressfully during peak 
hours. High punctuality will ensure customer satisfaction and provide a reliable means 
of public transport and connecting infrastructure. The automated system will also be 
able to offer longer operating hours in a metro-like operation, driving passenger growth 
at times that cannot be targeted today due to the reliance on human drivers. All of these 
points are in line with DSBs passenger satisfaction goals and meet the demands for future 
mobility in the Greater Copenhagen area. DSB also expects to gain economic benefits 
from the project. While the initial investment in technology, infrastructure upgrades and 
system integration is high, the company aims to reduce operating costs in the long term, 
which will result in a more cost-effective system. These lower marginal costs are one of 
the driving factors that allow for higher frequencies in the system. With higher passenger 
satisfaction and expected growth in passenger numbers, ticket sales will also increase 
to generate more revenue. Another factor not directly considered by DSB, as the system 
already operates with fully electric trains, is that it provides a very sustainable solution 
for travel in Copenhagen. [11] Sustainability can be seen as an argument in favour of 
switching to driverless trains. The increased frequency and reliability of the trains will 
help encourage people to use them instead of their cars to get around the Danish capital.
However, a project of this magnitude faces several challenges. The most significant 
of these is the implementation into an existing and already operating rail system. 
Unlike building a new metro system from scratch, converting an active commuter rail 
system must be done with minimal disruption to passengers and within the existing 
infrastructure.  To meet this challenge, DSB is planning to automate Line F first, which runs 
fairly independently of the other lines in the system. The plan is to use this line as a test 
bed for technology and operations. A major challenge is that many of the 87 stations on 
the network are very different in design. Safety is another, as the S-train system mostly 
runs in easily accessible areas, increasing the need for effective detection and automatic 
emergency systems. The Copenhagen metro has already shown that stations without 
platform screen doors can be a major challenge. Another issue is public acceptance and 
the change in workforce that automation brings.
DSB‘s plan to entirely automate the Copenhagen S-Train system is laid out in a few stages. 
In the first part, the current rolling stock will be upgraded to driverless technology. Later, 
new rolling stock, currently under development, will be introduced that is optimised and 
designed for driverless operation. [19]
==PAGE:28==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
027
3.4 INTERVIEWS
The introduction of a fully automated S-Train system is not only a technical challenge, 
but also brings about profound changes for passengers. In order to provide a sound 
basis for a user-friendly and inclusive station redesign, it is necessary to understand the 
behaviour, needs and challenges of passengers and how they interact with the station. 
Relying on specifications, regulations and a purely theoretical analysis of the problem 
is not enough. Such an approach often overlooks real problems and fails to identify the 
potential for improvement for passengers.
In order to ensure that the relevant problems and optimisation potentials at the S-Train 
station are analysed and explored in detail, it is logical for this project to choose a 
research approach that interacts directly with the affected stakeholders. Therefore, 
direct communication with and observation of passengers, the environment and people 
with relevant knowledge in the field of public transport and inclusive urban design is 
necessary. In this way it is possible to gain insights into the perspectives and identify not 
only the more obvious, but also the individual and hidden challenges that different users 
and groups face in real life. There are different approaches to obtaining such insights, 
each with different requirements and outcomes.
Thanks to the close cooperation with the DSB, but also with the DTU Skylab trough 
the Technology Leaving Now One Behind initiative, this project has access to a large 
network of experts who have already dealt with the topic of accessibility and inclusion in 
public spaces in various ways, but also with the technical requirements and behavioural 
patterns of passengers at the DSB in particular. By speaking with these people, the 
results and structures that have already been developed in this field, can be exchanged 
and recognised, and personal opinions for useful solutions and areas can be discussed. 
A lot of discussions with DSB employees from various areas, who are all involved in the 
future S-Train project, were held at the beginning of the project. These included people 
mainly involved with the safety and technical systems required for automation, but also 
people involved with passenger services and marketing. These interviews focused on 
the goals and direction of the project for the DSB, as well as where the project could be 
improved, particularly in terms of redesigning stations to make them more passenger-
friendly. These discussions were used to define a more precise scope of work and to align 
certain ideas and visions. These discussions provided a great deal of insight, which was 
particularly important at the beginning of the project and contributed to the direction 
and focus of the project. It became clear, for example, that large DSB teams are already 
dealing with the regulations and that a free, yes, design approach would be helpful.
Because public transport is so diverse, it is helpful to talk to people outside the 
organisation who have extensive knowledge in the field. Discussions were also held with 
the Copenhagen Metro, focusing in particular on the design approach the company has 
taken to designing stations for the metro, but also for the Copenhagen tram, which is 
due to open soon. This was of particular interest as these stations have been completely 
redesigned in contrast to the S-Train, which will use the existing stations. (Appendix 7.1.2) 
This provided some interesting insights, for example, into how the position of waiting 
areas or the size of platforms is defined. It was also very helpful to get an insight into the 
topic from an architectural point of view.
==PAGE:29==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
028
3.5 TRAIN STATION
Train stations are the point of intersection between passengers, the train, other modes of 
transport and the city. They fulfil a variety of functions that go far beyond simply allowing 
passengers to board and alight from trains. They are often an integral part of urban life 
and often reflect a city‘s identity. The Metro of Copenhagen designed its stations in a 
way to: “blend in with and enhance its urban surroundings – both in the existing city and 
in the new neighbourhoods.“ [20] Train stations can also provide space for retail, dining 
and cultural activities.
Designing a station or elements of it always requires a holistic approach. The needs 
of passengers and the operational requirements of trains must be considered as key 
factors. Important factors to consider when designing a platform station are accessibility, 
safety and legibility. As the place where the train is boarded and the mode of transport 
is accessed, the station should be as convenient and simple as possible, a place where 
passengers feel comfortable and can start or end their journey without any major 
problems.
The design of a new station, or the renovation of an existing one, is like a big jigsaw puzzle, 
with many different pieces that fit together to form a complete picture. If you ask most 
people, aesthetics is the most important part of station design. Architectural elements 
such as imposing half-domes or large glass roofs are striking and memorable. But there 
is another aspect that, if done well, remains invisible to the passenger: the design 
work that goes into making the passenger‘s journey as smooth as possible. [21] These 
factors have a huge impact on their journey and can determine whether they make 
a similar journey again or choose a different mode of transport. As station changes 
often involve large financial investments, planning is also important. It is important 
to include design in order to ultimately create a profitable system. Often publicly 
funded or even operated, rail systems and stations serve the general population as 
a whole. Their design should be geared to making them accessible to all. Through 
good design, stations can be integrated into the public image and culture of the 
people. This has long-term benefits for acceptance. It can also create sustainability 
in the city by attracting more passengers to travel by train. This is not only about 
environmental sustainability, but also about the social and societal sustainability 
that is central to today‘s society. [21]
If this aspect of social design 
is neglected, and all that is 
visible to the public is the grand 
architectural concepts, this can 
lead to great discontent with 
projects, as passengers see the 
railway as an everyday object 
rather than a prestige project. 
There is a very prominent example 
of this in Germany, where the city 
of Stuttgart has been rebuilding its 
main station for decades, despite 
protests. [22] This social aspect 
must therefore also be taken 
into account when designing and 
analysing such projects.

3.5.1 THE CURRENT S-TRAIN STATION
The station is a very important aspect of any journey on the S-Train, because this is 
where the journey begins. Passengers come from home and work to start or end their 
journey by train, or they change to another mode of transport. This leads to a strong 
interaction with the passenger and the existing infrastructure, which is why the risk of 
misunderstandings and confusion is high.
to create the most effective solution for more passenger guidance and better inclusivity 
within the stations. With the switch to autonomous trains, the requirements placed on 
stations are increasing, but as the existing building substance and infrastructure is very 
different, it would be too much work and costly to tackle each station individually, but it 
is not possible to design a standardised solution that can be applied effectively to every 
station.
DSB works with a large team of experts and engineers to configure and modify the stations 
for driverless trains. This team is mainly focussing on installing the necessary technical 
and safety systems for the use of autonomous trains in the existing stations. However, 
this project looks relatively little beyond the necessary systems and introduces solutions 
for greater efficiency, passenger experience and inclusion. Addressing these points, 
however, can provide great advantages for the later acceptance and effectiveness of a 
self-driving urban railway system.
Copenhagen‘s S-Train network has grown with the city over time, and this is reflected in 
the stations on the system. As they always reflect local conditions and requirements, the 
87 stations are not all the same and can vary immensely in design. The period in which 
the stations were built varies greatly, as does the existing infrastructure. This has a 
significant impact on the passenger experience and is a major challenge for standardised 
solutions across the system. These challenges must be analysed and recognised in order

The entire S-Train network in Copenhagen currently consists 
of 87 stations and is being viewed in this way for the automation 
of the system. In the future, a few more stations will be added 
in the direction of Roskilde. The general layout is important for 
understanding the individual stations. DSB distinguishes between 
side platforms and island platforms. In the 10 side platforms, the 
platforms are separate from each other, outside the tracks, while 
the tracks run through the centre. This often results in narrower 
platforms. The 10 island platforms have the platform in the centre, 
surrounded by tracks on either side. These stations are often wider, 
but have a higher passenger density as both directions share a 
platform. [Internal Data]
FIGURE 16: Station Overview
==PAGE:32==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
031
3.5.2 FEATURES
There are already a number of systems and infrastructure solutions in place at 
Copenhagen S-Train stations that enable or ease travelling for passengers. Many different 
systems are already in use, particularly in the area of information provision. There are 
digital screens that display the train line and final destination, screens that provide an 
overview of the next trains and speaker announcements for unforeseen changes in the 
operating schedule. There is also a large amount of written information provided by signs 
such as station names or track numbers. The entire S-Train network, rules of behaviour 
and timetables are also displayed in writing. These information systems are based on 
the corporate identity. The DSB and future solutions should be developed in this style. 
 
There are also features on the platform for other purposes. For example, there are 
painted bicycles, which are intended to serve as orientation for passengers with bicycles. 
However, these are not very flexible and are often inaccurate for different train lengths. 
There are also not lifts or tactile guidance systems for the blind at every station. Security 
cameras or extensive waiting areas, infrastructure such as benches or covered shelters 
also vary greatly from station to station. Overall, it is clear that the existing infrastructure 
can fulfil many important functions. Nevertheless, there is still a need for optimisation in 
some areas in order to make the service more effective and inclusive. This is particularly 
true in view of the forthcoming automation.
Current station renovation projects are already incorporating more elements of inclusive 
station design through DSB and other companies involved. For example, Carlsberg 
station has been renovated in recent years and new lifts have been installed. [23] A 
comprehensive tactile guidance system for blind passengers has also been installed.

3.5.3 OBSERVATIONS
A useful and established method of analysing passenger behaviour is to observe 
passengers in their normal setting. Through unobtrusive observation, typical movement 
patterns, difficulties and potential for optimisation can be identified without passengers 
distorting their behaviour by knowing that they are in a scientific study and therefore 
behaving normally. This also allows the interaction between passengers and the various 
elements of the day on the platform to be easily identified.
Observation offers several advantages for obtaining relevant and useful results for the 
project. By unobtrusively observing users in their everyday and natural use of the S-Train 
and the station, the actual behaviour of the users can be identified. This is an advantage 
as surveys or interviews often contain subjective statements based on perceptions 
and are often not well thought out. This analysis of real behaviour makes it possible to 
identify hidden problems and behaviours that the users themselves are not aware of. 
Of particular relevance to the project, for example, is the way passengers move around 
the station and their waiting habits. Another advantage of this method is that a very 
large and, above all, natural user group is analysed and observed. Real passengers using 
the S-Train are very important for the project, as they provide the most realistic results. 
During station observations at a natural time, these passengers are found and observed. 
These include commuters, tourists or passengers with disabilities such as the elderly, 
people with disabilities or school classes, each with different behaviours and needs.
In order to obtain the best possible quality of results, it is important to observe the 
stations with well defined factors and also to change these factors in order to detect 
correlations or differences. For this project, both the station and the time of day were 
precisely recorded and varied. This allows a wide range of user behaviour to be identified. 
For example, differences between busy stations during rush hour or after special events 
compared to less busy stations at lunchtime. It also makes sense to monitor Copenhagen‘s 
metro stations, as they have a different infrastructure but ultimately serve the same 
purpose of providing space for passengers to wait and use the public transport system. 
It is essential that the results are recorded as accurately as possible in order to obtain 
usable results. These can not only be used for further research, but also as an argument 
and point of reference during the following design process.
For this project, observations were made at different S-Train stations at different times of 
the day. All these factors were recorded in detail in order to enable traceability. During the 
observation, specific events that took place on the platform were recorded. (Appendix 
7.2) It did not matter how exactly these events were relevant to the project. This created 
a realistic overview that was not targeted at a specific problem. General findings were 
also noted for each observation. Generally these are not related to a specific problem, 
but can combine different events and situations. These general observations give a very 
rough picture of passenger behaviour. It is therefore necessary to evaluate and analyse 
these findings in the further course of the design process.
This observation revealed many interesting aspects. In particular, patterns were identified 
in the way passengers behaved while waiting. These are helpful in defining and developing 
solutions as the project progresses.
==PAGE:34==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
033
3.5.4 METRO STATION
It‘s not necessary to search far to discover how passengers behave in a station of a 
system already operating with autonomous trains. The entire metro system of the city 
of Copenhagen runs with driverless trains, and therefore has some design points and 
technical solutions in the stations that are focused on this environment.
The use of autonomous trains and precise sensor technology means that the trains 
have precise stopping points at the stations, ensuring that the exact same points of 
entry and exit for apssengers are used at all times. This is also necessary as there are 
automatic platform screening doors throughout the system. These open at the same 
time as the train doors, allowing passengers to disembark and embark, while at the same 
time screening off the track area and providing a safe waiting area for passengers when no 
train is in the station. This system was not in place at the metro‘s above-ground stations 
when they opened, causing many problems and operational delays. These problems 
were so severe that “the detection system at the metro‘s 12 above-ground stations will 
now be replaced by Automatic Platform Gates (APG)“. [24] As well as providing safety 
for passengers, platform doors have further effects. Observations(Appendix 7.2.4)have 
shown that the glass walls and platform doors, especially in the underground stations 
of the metro, give a feeling of safety, so that passengers often stand close to these 
walls or even lean against them. This contradicts the usual behaviour of passengers 
in stations without this system, where this waiting point would be the closest point to 
the dangerous track. The metro has recognised this behaviour and is using additional 
markings to make boarding and alighting more efficient.  White waiting lines have been 
painted on the floor to the right and left of the doors, signalling to passengers where 
they can wait to board the next train. This feature has been very well received and used 
by passengers. In particular, it has improved waiting behaviour. However, there are some 
remaining problems with boarding as the sideways position of waiting passengers makes 
it difficult to see into the open door to see if passengers are about to disembark, which 
can lead to situations where passengers get in each other‘s way.
Another interesting aspect related to the doors is the use of tactile infrastructure directly 
in front of the doors. A field of metal dots is installed in the doorway, signalling to blind 
people with canes where the doors are located, allowing them to enter the train. This is a 
good example of how a solution for one group of passengers affects others. Observation 
has shown that no passengers wait in these 
positions, leaving the area in front of the 
doors free. It has also shown that waiting 
at these tactile points is not unpleasant, 
but it is noticeable, so that passengers 
subconsciously decide to leave the area. 
In general, the metro stations have a good, 
tactile infrastructure, although the above-
ground station has a much more extensive 
infrastructure due to the later addition 
of the platform doors, which also lead to 
ticket machines and emergency counters. 
(Appendix 7.4.1) Another advantage of the 
metro station is that it was designed from 
the outset to be level free, which allows 
passengers with wheelchairs or Strollers 
to easily roll onto the trains without having 
to overcome large edges or gaps.

3.6 PASSENGER
It is essential in the design process to talk to the future users of the solution, particularly 
on matters such as inclusivity. There needs to be a deep understanding of how passengers 
experience the station. Accompanying people with disabilities on one of their journeys 
is very helpful as it provides valuable insights into the everyday challenges they face 
when travelling by S-Train. These insights are difficult to capture in other ways, such as 
surveys or general assumptions. 
The challenges that people with disabilities face on a daily basis are often overlooked 
by people involved in such a project who do not live with a disability. In order to 
identify some of these challenges, it is important to follow people on their journey and 
identify difficulties and vulnerabilities in everyday and normal use. This helps to better 
understand user behaviour and develop targeted improvements that can then benefit 
everyone, as designing for people with disabilities leads to better design for everyone. 
When accompanying people with disabilities on their journeys, it is important not only to 
observe problems, but also to learn from them in order to adapt solutions to make public 
transport accessible and functional. 
For this thesis, two passengers were accompanied on their journey on the Copenhagen 
S-Train. This was done in as natural an environment as possible, and problems and 
challenges were discussed and analysed during a normal commuter train journey. Both of 
these people use the S-Train regularly and the contact was established through Skylab 
and the “Technology leaving no one behind“ initiative. (Appendix 7.3)
The general needs and behaviour of passengers are essential in order to ultimately 
develop a relevant solution that can be applied by users. There are a number of things 
that most passengers want, and these are often relatively basic when it comes to 
travelling by train. Many passengers expect public transport to be a cheap and reliable 
mode of transport that gets them to their destination safely. [25] In addition, there are 
other requirements that may vary from passenger to passenger. These expectations are 
mainly related to the passenger‘s skills and motivation. If some of these expectations 
are not met, the passenger‘s behaviour at the station or on the train will change and this 
may cause delays in the system.
Passenger behaviour also needs to be examined, especially in the case of autonomous 
systems. Has the system been well accepted? Because, unlike the metro, it is not 
currently feasible to install platform doors in an S-Train station. This allows direct access 
to the system and direct interaction between passenger and train. This may lead to some 
behavioural issues that could be disruptive to the autonomous operation of the system. 
For example, autonomous cars in the US are often found to be sabotaged or tampered with 
by people. [26] This is often not done with the direct intention of destroying things, but 
the vehicles, which are generally seen as a shared asset, make it easy to attack because 
there is no direct policing. In addition, many people find it entertaining to discover the 
weaknesses of these systems and then manipulate them. This kind of behaviour will 
also be relevant for the passengers of the S-Train in the future. What opportunities for 
manipulation do passengers have in the station and how can they be countered, is an 
important question to be asked.
Understanding the passenger and their needs and capabilities is therefore essential to 
the project in order to develop meaningful solutions. Ideally, the passenger, as the main 
user of the system, is involved in the design process.
==PAGE:36==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
035
3.6.1 DSBs S-TRAIN PASSENGER
The DSB has a wealth of passenger data and information that provides valuable insights 
into passenger behaviour, usage and decision making in relation to its services. This data 
enables a better understanding of passengers and their needs. This is not only important 
for optimising the existing system and its economic value, but can also be used in the 
design of the future autonomous S-train.
The switch to autonomous driverless trains represents a significant change in the 
capabilities and operation of Copenhagen‘s S-Train system. This will not only affect 
the technical operation of the system, but will also change passenger behaviour and 
expectations. These changes present DSB with both opportunities and obstacles 
that effect spesific passenger groups. The data that has already been collected and 
summarised in relation to passengers for a future S-Train network is extremely important. 
Only if the needs of passengers are understood and actively involved in the design of a 
new system, and especially in the redesign of stations, can acceptance and usage be 
achieved and, if used well, new target groups and opportunities can be reached.
The passenger data collected also plays a central role as part of this project. It serves 
as a basis for understanding the different user groups. It helps to find out which spesific 
passenger groups travel by train and for what reasons, and which groups do not. Is it 
possible to address these needs by redesigning the station to make the journey more 
attractive or easier for passengers? If so, what specific needs and expectations do these 
passengers have for solutions that can be installed on the platform?
For DSB, passengers are the users of its services. This means that the passenger is also 
seen as the company‘s main source of revenue. However, it is important to note that the 
needs and requirements of passengers are not only seen from a financial perspective. 
This implies that investment in better infrastructure and inclusive services can be seen 
not only as short-term expenses, but as investments in a long-term and sustainable 
future. As a company with a public background, it is important to sometimes deviate a 
certain amount from the cost-benefit ratio in order to enable as many passengers as 
possible, especially the residents of the city of Copenhagen, to use the S-Train.
In order to represent all this data for their passengers and the potential use that DSB has, they have divided their passengers into six different categories. These categories describe 
various patterns of use and behaviour. They also show the background to why passengers behave and use the train the way they do. A lot can be learned from this data, especially when it 
comes to highlighting the potential and reasons for using or not using the products and public transport. From this, possible solutions can be derived that would lead to a higher use of the 
service by the specific user group. Aligning this with the goals of the S-Train automation and station redesign opportunities to reach new target groups can be created. [Internal Data]

MASTERTHESIS

==PAGE:38==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
037
In view of the forthcoming switch to self-driving trains, these data provide some interesting 
results. As DSB expects economic benefits and improvements from the project as a 
whole, and passengers are the main source of income for the company, the potential in 
passengers is great. Generally speaking, the passenger groups can be divided into two 
distinct segments. [Internal Data]
The first consists of passengers who use DSB‘s services on a regular basis. This customer 
segment is therefore already being catered for. Therefore, no significant transformation 
is required to exploit the potential that lies within this group. For example the Bylister 
already sees the S-Train as a useful and inviting means of transport for their own 
journeys, but is prevented from making all journeys on the S-Train by limitations in the 
timetable or problems in the operation. DSB hopes that automation will increase the 
reliability and frequency of the trains, which will meet some of the expectations of this 
type of passenger. The expectations of Mobilister, which is already using the S-Train 
but sometimes switches to other modes of transport for reasons of time and efficiency, 
go in a similar direction. This market can also be better serviced by increasing capacity, 
efficiency and reliability. The last passenger in this segment is the Kollektivister who 
already uses public transport for the most part. The potential for growth in this group 
is relatively small, but care must be taken to ensure that these passengers are not lost 
in the long term. For example, it is important to continue to provide options such as free 
bicycle transport on the S-Train or special ticket offers. Due to the reorganisation of 
S-Bahn operations and the three-station design, only relatively small increases in market 
share can be achieved in this segment, making it all the more important to retain these 
people through targeted solutions. This leads to a predictable and consistent revenue 
stream. 
The other segment of passengers is those who, for a variety of reasons, do not use 
DSB regularly. This segment of passengers is much more difficult to address and in 
most cases requires such large investments and restructuring of the existing system 
to meet their needs and requirements. However, there is great potential for growth 
in this segment due to the high market share and relatively low use of DSB services 
to date. The greatest investment is needed to convince local people to travel with 
DSB. As there is often no public transport infrastructure at the place of residence, 
the network would have to be significantly expanded in order to convince this type 
of passenger to use it regularly. For the S-Train, however, this is not so important, 
as this group is happy to use services when they are on a trip in Copenhagen. 
Automating the S-Train would not change much for this type of passenger. The Bil-
Idealister has the highest market share and therefore great potential for improvement 
and for attracting new potential customers. However, it is very difficult to convince 
this group of passengers to use public transport and the S-Train. For this group, public 
transport is a slow and unreliable way to travel, due to their strong financial position and 
fixed opinions. The car is often used as a means of transport. Automation of the S-Train 
can utilise part of this potential through the hoped-for higher frequency and efficiency. 
The benefits would have to be clearly communicated and accepted by this group of 
passengers. This would be a long process, but gains can be achieved through sustained 
improvements to the system. The last type of passenger, the Vane-Bilister, often sees 
using public transport as a major effort. Older people in particular have problems with 
this and find travelling by train challenging and not inclusive. The potential in this group 
is great and will grow in the future due to the demographic shift in Denmark. [27] By 
redesigning the S-Train and stations in a sensible and inclusive way, the potential in 
this group can be significantly realised with moderate investment, which is particularly 
beneficial for future development.
Overall, it is possible to recognise some key points in which the automation of the S-Train 
can offer advantages for DSB‘s future passenger development. In particular, existing 
and new customers can be attracted by the expected higher frequency, reliability and 
efficiency of the system. In addition, it can be seen that an inclusive design in the trains 
and stations will maximise these benefits and make it easier for passengers to use the 
system in general, leading to possible growth. This is particularly important in terms of 
long-term planning and profitability.
 
==PAGE:39==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS

038
PASSENGERS WITHOUT PROBLEMS
More than half of the passengers using the S-Train 
in Copenhagen experience no problems during their 
journey. While they don‘t need special accommodations, 
clear signage and optimised station flow can improve 
their travel experience. As the main concern for this 
group of people is their comfort, such as the frequency of 
trains and punctuality, there are also points of attention 
and improvement that DSB needs to consider.
Not all passengers on the S-Train experience the journey in the same way. While many navigate the stations with ease, a significant proportion of passengers face challenges during 
their journey. Understanding the needs of all passengers and what causes their problems plays a crucial role in the design of stations, trains and operations.  Finding solutions to this is 
necessary to provide accessible public transport for all in the city. For DSB, it is also crucial from a business perspective, as serving a wide population creates the largest possible customer 
potential. It is also important as many people rely on public transport in their daily lives, and leaving a significant group out of the focus can cause social challenges. By looking at the 45% 
of passengers who experience difficulties during their journey on the S-Train, many target points and problems can be identified, which can then be targeted to find a suitable solution. 
By solving these difficulties, all passengers will benefit from a better working system. [Internal Data]
55%
35%
10%
PASSENGERS WITH SOME PROBLEMS
35% of passengers experience some kind of problem 
during their journey, with a wide range of issues that 
can affect these passengers. Temporary disability 
is like an injury, situational difficulties such as rush 
hourtraffic, navigating unfamiliar stations and complex 
ticketing systems can be a factor. In order to improve 
the experience of these passengers, clear signage 
and data communication, as well as working station 
infrastructure, are key elements.
PASSENGERS WITH MAJOR PROBLEMS
10% of passengers on the Copenhagen S-Train network 
face significant barriers due to disabilities and special 
needs. These passengers often have severe disabilities, 
such as blindness or wheelchair usage. For this group of 
passengers, small obstacles, such as a broken elevator, 
can make train journeys impossible. This group of 
passengers often rely on public transport to get around 
the city, so taking their problems and accessibility needs 
into account is an important factor for DSB.
