3.6.4 RESULTS
By closely observing the situation and making contact with various relevant people, it was possible to achieve several results related to the focus of the project. These could be of interest 
for a project related to the redesign of the S-Train stations. The focus of the project was to address problems that the planned work of the DSB would not be solving. From this, we can 
draw some conclusions about the passenger‘s problems in relation to stations and activities. These problems describe situations that often go unnoticed and therefore receive little 
attention in the design process. Even small situations can have an impact on the passenger experience and, in unfavourable situations, can become major problems.
PASSENGER
Some clear points emerged from the passengers‘ follow 
around. There are many dangers for the blind passenger 
on the platform, and despite the existing infrastructure 
for assistance, the blind person still needs direct 
contact and help from other passengers. When there 
are no other passengers,the journey becomes almost 
impossible for the person. Finding the doors was a big 
problem, because there‘s often no audio signal, and 
if no one‘s getting off, the door doesn‘t make a sound 
either. It was also interesting to see that the blind 
person had to go an extra stop to change trains. Non-
blind people would not have recognised this behaviour. 
This is because the infrastructure at the next station 
is much better, making it much easier to change trains. 
There were also a lot of lessons to be learned from the 
other passengers. Probably the most important was 
how this person felt about each station. It was clearly 
stated that this station is very good and the other one 
is really bad.
STATION
When observing the situation in the station, it was 
particularly clear that much of the existing infrastructure 
is not used by many passengers. This suggests that 
passengers do not pay enough attention to where they 
can get the right information. On the other hand, the 
information sources are often not placed in the best 
visibility position. The result is that passengers do not 
look for information and do not find it when they do. 
This leads to delays, especially for waiting passengers 
who board the train not knowing whether they are on 
the right train or in the wrong place. It also highlighted 
the diversity of passengers‘ needs and circumstances. 
Some people are very active users of the S-Train, while 
others do not use it at all. Developing a solution that 
suits everyone is a big challenge..
INTERACTION
Many interesting facts emerge from the interaction 
between passengers and stations. One of these is the 
relationship between passengers and information, 
which has already been mentioned. On the other 
hand, it is very interesting to observe the behaviour 
of passengers while they are waiting. For example, 
passengers often queue next to each other rather 
than in front of and behind each other. There may be 
several reasons for this, one of which is likely to be a 
sense of safety. However, a lot of usable space in the 
station is lost because of passenger behaviour. With 
the automation of trains, it would therefore make sense 
to address these issues. It also shows very clearly that 
many people do not move far around the station. This 
often leads to congestion, especially around entrances 
and exits, which prevents other passengers from moving 
freely around the station.y.

3.7 EXISTING 
3.7.1 INFORMATION
One of the major focus points for railway companies is the way in which information is 
communicated to passengers. There are a lot of options for the information that needs 
to be communicated. In general, it is remarkable how much information the passenger 
has to process when travelling by train, from signs indicating the name of the station or 
the track number, to screens showing the train and direction, or by announcements in 
unforeseen situations such as track changes or emergencies. The important thing here 
is how the passenger receives this information and whether it is communicated clearly. 
For all these points, there are various systems and solutions that aim to provide targeted 
and comprehensible information.
DISPLAYS
Swiss Federal Railways (SBB) uses a centralised content management system to provide 
customer information consistently across various channels, including digital signage. 
The displays are designed to be easy to read, even for visually impaired people, with clear 
contrasts and large lettering. [28]
With the Smart Information Displays (SID), SBB not only wants to make paper posters 
unnecessary, but also offer its customers interactive information in real time. This is 
FIGURE 26: Interactive screen
In order to make effective and robust design decisions in the later part of the design 
process, it is important to analyse existing solutions in the context of railway stations. 
A comprehensive overview of which solutions are already being implemented 
worldwide and how they are proving themselves in practice makes it possible to identify 
approaches that might be useful for this project and also to avoid mistakes that have 
already been made elsewhere. In this way, solutions can be further developed on 
the basis of innovations that have already been developed in relation to this project 
by adapting them to the current situation. Aspects such as security ticket option, 
waiting areas code, information transfer to passengers play a central role and have 
already been tried to improve through many projects and applications. The aim is to 
identify these applications and solutions and evaluate them with regard to the project. 
 
From an academic perspective, it is also important to find interesting approaches that 
deal with design principles or the precise analysis of passenger behaviour and passenger 
flow, for example. These scientific findings can provide a valuable basis for developing 
evidence-based solutions and building on research results. Overall, it is important to 
look at and scrutinise the existing field of existing solutions. This can deliver important 
results in the context of automation.

made possible by a solution based on AEM screens. The SIDs are controlled centrally 
and customers receive the information they need. Thanks to the touchscreen function, 
customers can call up additional details about their journey, such as information about 
the train‘s carriages or shopping facilities at the station. This works via widgets on the 
home screen. In special situations, such as signalling  disruptions, information can be 
transmitted in real time. The SIDs are a real relief for people with disabilities and mobility 
impairments. They can have all the information read out to them and wheelchair users can 
adjust the height of the content displayed. SBB has therefore developed its own central 
dashboard. Here, those responsible can see at a glance which posters are relevant for 
which station. [29]

SIGNAGE
There are a variety of approaches that can be adopted at railway stations in order to 
facilitate journeys for impaired individuals. One such option is the implementation of 
barrier-free signage. This approach involves the design of signs that are characterised 
by clear, legible fonts and high-contrast colours for example, white text on a dark 
background, with the objective of optimising visibility. [30]
Another option that may be considered is the use of tactile elements and Braille lettering 
on signs in addition to the visual representation. The purpose of this would be to enable 
visually impaired people to grasp the information using their sense of touch. [31]
In addition to the aforementioned systems, there are also combined acoustic-visual 
systems. In these systems, visual signs are supplemented by integrated acoustic 
information. Sensors or manual triggers activate acoustic announcements that 
reproduce the content of the signs, thereby creating an additional level of information. 
At Tokyo Station, there is a sophisticated system of information displays that uses high 
contrast colours, large fonts and integrated audible cues. [32] The visual displays provide 
important information such as platform numbers and departure times in real time in an 
easily legible form. In addition, acoustic announcements and tactile elements ensure 
that visually impaired travellers can also find their way independently and process these 
infromations.
SPEAKERS & ANNOUNCMENTS
Announcements and loudspeaker announcements play an important role in helping 
people with disabilities to find their way around station buildings. The following section 
discusses some of the ways in which these methods can be used effectively to achieve 
this goal. [33] Firstly, it is important to use clear, simple and slow language. For people 
with hearing impairments, loudspeaker announcements should be clear to understand. 
Another factor are multilingual announcments, which serve tourists.  This approach also 
makes it easier for people with learning difficulties to understand. In addition, visual aids 
such as monitors can be used to showcase the verbal information and thus improve 
accessibility and comprehensibility. For example, information can be displayed on digital 
screens in simple language or in sign language.
In Japan, for instance, the ‚Ekimatopeia‘ system employs artificial intelligence to visualise 
the annoucmets in text and sign language. [34]
FIGURE 27: Adaptable UI
==PAGE:46==

TACTILE INFRASTRUCTURE
Tactile floor strips play a huge part in helping people who are visually impaired move 
around safely and independently. Floor indicators are structural components that can 
be detected by individuals with severe visual impairment who utilise a long cane, due to 
their tactile contrast to the surrounding surface. The tactile, visual and, if necessary, 
acoustic nature of floor indicators enables them to alert individuals to potential hazards, 
provide orientation and guidance. There are two different types of tactile infrastructure 
on the ground of platforms. They differ in what they aim to achieve. One is to guide the 
blind passenger in directions through the station and the other is to indicate hazards 
and dangerous areas.
MAPS
Tactile maps are an aid that 
enables 
people 
with 
visual 
impairments to find their way 
around complex  environments 
such as railway stations. The 
tactile information is shown 
using raised lines and includes 
paths, buildings and facilities. 
This 
tactile 
information 
is 
supplemented by Braille labelling, 
which enables targeted and 
independent 
navigation. 
One 
example is the following from a 
railway station in Warsaw, Poland.
[35]
The following elements can be distinguished with regard to guidance and orientation: 
Guidance strips, directional fields, turn-off fields, location strips, entry fields, attention 
fields, attention strips and barrier fields. The term location strip refers to an area whose 
purpose is to provide orientation for destinations that are primarily on the side. The 
surface is usually laid across the width of the pavement or footpath and is characterised 
by a ribbed structure. The detection strips are utilised for orientation in relation to 
destinations to the side of the primary walking direction, such as bus stops, stairs or 
the initiation of guidance strips or guidance systems. Attention fields are defined as 
surfaces or strips with a studded structure that indicate changes in level, the termination 
of a walking area, hazards and obstacles. Square areas with a dimpled structure, so-
called junction fields, typically function in conjunction with guidance strips or detection 
strips as indicators for junctions and bends. Boarding bays are surfaces with a ribbed 
structure that runs parallel to the board and are used to mark the boarding point on 
public transport. Directional fields are used to indicate the direction of travel at crossing

areas.
3.7.2 TICKETING
Ticketing at the station goes beyond simply purchasing a ticket and includes a variety 
of aspects to ensure that all travellers - especially people with disabilities - have access 
to an efficient, user-friendly and accessible ticket purchasing process. Ticket vending 
machines are widely used and offer a quick way to buy tickets. They are often equipped 
with touchscreens and selection menus. These machines should offer several functions 
tailored to visual impairments, hearing impairments and walking disabilities to ensure 
the independence and comfort of passengers. In Switzerland, travellers with a visual 
impairment can request assistance in operating a ticket machine by telephone. The 
necessary information can be found on the start screen of the machine. The relevant 
contact centre provides support 24 hours a day and can control the machine remotely 
and prepare the ticket.      
FIGURE 31: Wheelchair friendly screen

3.7.3 WAITING
Waiting behaviour has a major impact on how passengers interact with the station and 
how they evaluate the experience once they have completed their journey. As waiting to 
board a train is generally seen as a waste of time, passengers will often try to make this time 
as comfortable as possible or position themselves in the best position to board. There are 
several approaches and solutions to influence or improve passengers‘ waiting behaviour. 
 
SEATING
There are a variety of places where passengers can wait in the station. There are often 
different types of seating available to pass the time. The more local the mode of transport, 
the less this resource is used in stations. For example, many metro companies decide to 
install benches that are used for short periods of time but are not comfortable to use 
in the long term. These projects often attract public criticism, as many people see the 
inhospitable design as discriminating. [39] The aim is to discourage people from spending 
long periods in underground stations. There are currently a variety of benches or shelters 
in Copenhagen‘s S-Train stations, which are also intended to provide protection from 
the weather.
LIGHT/INDECATION
The position where passengers wait is crucial 
to the efficiency of boarding and alighting. 
There are a number of systems that take this 
into account. In Taipei, for example, LED strips 
are mounted above the boarding doors and 
use four colours - green, yellow, orange and 
red - to indicate the level of occupancy of the 
incoming train, thus influencing passengers‘
FIGURE 33: Screen in Berlin
Wheelchair users should be able to reach the screen and controls at an easily accessible 
height. The machine can have an adjustable or fixed height for the display and controls. 
Tokyo Station, which is operated by the TOEI transport company, is equipped with ticket 
vending machines with a low height. Switzerland also has machiens like this. 
The automatic ticketing system of the Shanghai Metro includes voice-operated ticket 
vending machines (TVM) [38]:The ticket vending machines are equipped with voice 
recognition, which enables passengers to specify their destinations by voice commands. 
There are three modes: voice query, spelling query and touch screen selection. To 
improve the accuracy of voice recognition in noisy environments such as underground 
stations, a combination of microphone arrays and cameras is used, the exact position 
of passengers is determined by facial recognition, and noise cancellation is based on 
microphone array technology.

Light can also be used to highlight active or less active parts of the station or to make 
them less apparent to passengers. Projections using spotlights can be used to provide 
directional and informational guidance to passengers on the platform. [41] The big 
advantage is that spotlights are flexible and can be switched on and off at short notice 
to draw attention to dynamic situations, such as a missing carriage or a short train and 
its stopping position.
 
ZONES
Another method of influencing passenger waiting behaviour is to create special waiting 
areas for passengers. In Taipei, for example, safe waiting areas have been introduced, with 
an increased presence of security staff and surveillance cameras, as well as the possibility 
of direct interaction with staff via intercom systems. This marked zone provides a safe 
waiting area, especially at night and during late hours. [42] Such zones are also helpful 
in assisting passengers with disabilities. In Japan, special areas for disabled passengers 
are implemented and permanently manned by staff who can help the person board and 
alight the train or find their way around the station. These zones are positioned so that 
when the train arrives, the disabled person is waiting in the right place and can easily 
board the train. 
These zones can be very helpful in guiding passengers, creating a better experience and 
increasing efficiency. However, it is important to consider the needs and requirements of 
passengers. In London, for example, a system called Green Lanes was trialled to indicate 
waiting areas for passengers and to provide a free path for passengers disembarking 
from trains. [43] Large green lines were placed on the platform. However, passengers 
found it very difficult to intuitively understand where the right spot to queue was located. 
Passengers often stood on the green lines as the colour is generally perceived as inviting 
and positive. However, the purpose of the green lines was to indicate paths that needed 
==PAGE:50==

049
3.7.5 SAFETY
Especially for autonomous train systems, it is important to implement safety systems 
that protect passengers from the dangers posed by trains.
BARRIERS
The most common means of providing safe platforms on modern high-speed systems 
are platform doors. These provide a permanent and continuous barrier between the 
track and the platform, preventing passengers from reaching the track when there is no 
train in the station. These systems are now widely used around the world and are highly 
effective. The technical sensors and rolling stock used in these systems are essential 
to ensure precise stopping positions and therefore smooth passenger boarding and 
alighting. When such a system is implemented as a completely closed system, it offers 
even more benefits. For example, the clear separation of stations and tracks can improve 
air conditioning, especially in underground stations, and reduce passenger exposure to 
particulate matter. [48]
FIGURE 35: Platform doors in London
FIGURE 36: Half platform doors in Tokyo
3.7.4 RETAIL
The fact that railway stations are frequented by many people passing through on a daily 
basis, and are often located in the heart of the city, means that they offer great potential 
for sales and comercial income. [45]  Goods such as food and beverages as well as 
souvenirs and other items are offered. Different types of shops are found. In large stations 
shops or even restaurants are permanent, while mobile kiosks or vending machines are 
used on the platforms themselves or in smaller stations. 
VENDING MACHINES
Vending machines at railway stations are designed to offer travellers quick and easy 
access to products and services. They are open 24/7 and have a wide range of products, 
from snacks and drinks to souvenirs and fresh meals. They can be stocked with different 
items depending on the location and target group. [46]
There are also unusual concepts. For example, at Tazawako station in Akita, Japan, 
there is a vending machine that sells bear meat. This has been available since 2023. The 
idea is to promote local specialities, and the machine is operated by a local restaurant. 
A sustainable idea is the ‘Rettomat’ at Frankfurt Central Station in Germany.  [47] The 
vending machine delivers organic and superfood snacks such as nuts or protein bars 
and drinks. The food is either surplus or just before or after the expiry date. The aim is to 
reduce food waste and offer travellers sustainable snack options. 
==PAGE:51==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS

050
3.7.6 RELATED WORK
Over the past few years, scientific research has intensively explored the topic of crowd 
control at railway stations. A recent study by Boomers et al. [50] explores the topic of 
crowd control at railway stations. The researchers conducted experiments involving 
approximately 1,000 participants as part of the Crowd Management in Transport 
Infrastructures (CroMa) project. The study aims to understand passenger behaviour 
at railway stations and develop effective strategies for managing crowds. Experimental 
data was collected in various scenarios, including tests on access management, boarding 
processes, and bottlenecks. The findings highlight the importance of access points and 
exit routes in crowd management. It is recommended to avoid bottlenecks and create 
separate access routes for different types of travellers, such as train passengers, 
commuters, and people with disabilities. The study also emphasizes the value of clear 
signage and visual cues in guiding travellers through the station safely and efficiently. 
The insights can inform the design of safer and more efficient station areas.
One of the key recommendations is that people with disabilities should be given special 
consideration in the planning and design of stations and crowd management systems. 
Stations should minimise barriers and ensure that passengers using wheelchairs, mobility 
aids or with visual or hearing impairments can travel safely and efficiently. In particular, 
accessibility features such as lifts, wheelchair ramps and tactile markings should be well 
located throughout the station and not blocked by other crowds.
Research in the field of accessibility in station design is also concerned with analysing 
the problems of the ‘gap’ between platform and train, especially for people with reduced 
mobility. Moug & Coxon [51] have analysed the infrastructural and operational challenges 
at Australian stations, such as Melbourne, with the aim of supporting the development 
of design solutions to promote independent use and technical compliance. The defined 
standards (DSAPT) provide measurable guidelines for ramps, handrails and materials to 
As well as fully enclosed and electronically controlled systems, there are also permanent 
barriers that can be installed at stations to protect passengers. These are often metal 
barriers or short fences that keep the door positions of the trains clear, but leave the rest 
of the track clear. This creates a safe area for passengers, and also provides a means of 
indicating of stop positions so that passengers understand where the trains are coming 
to a halt. Unused parts of the station or track can also be shielded and made safer, or 
better crowd management can be achieved in any of these areas through targeted 
measures, reducing the risk of accidents or overcrowding.
SAFTEY ZONES
Clear indication of the danger zone between the safe waiting area on the platform and 
the track is an important means of ensuring a safe station. It is now standard practice to 
provide some form of visual distinction of the safe waiting areas. Tactile information is 
also often used to clearly signal these areas to people with disabilities.
At Geelong Central Station in Australia, safety zones have been created on the platforms 
as part of recent renovations. [49] Each platform has yellow floor markings that clearly 
indicate the safety zones with lines and lettering. Floor indicators lead to these areas as 
ribbed guide strips in the direction of travel, and are easily identifiable from the ground 
thanks to their reddish colour. 
The zones are wide enough to 
accommodate wheelchair users. 
Each of these safety zones has 
an emergency call system with 
red emergency call buttons. 
There are also information signs, 
for example with the route map. 
The safety zones are monitored 
by cameras when the station is 
open.

3.8 SDGs
The United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) are a universal framework 
that outlines the most important global challenges facing humanity and the planet. 
This framework is designed as a collaborative tool to understand, analyse and address 
these global challenges. The aim is to promote accountability and shape outcomes that 
ensure benefits for all, everywhere. They are designed to apply to and bring together 
organisations from different areas. This includes governments, academic institutions, 
companies and public organisations.
They are at the heart of the “2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development, adopted by 
all United Nations Member States in 2015“. [53] The aim is for all countries, developed 
and developing, to work together on projects to “improve health and education, reduce 
inequality, and spur economic growth – all while tackling climate change and working to 
preserve our oceans and forests.“ [53] 
Design projects that focus on innovation can greatly benefit from looking into these 17 
Sustainable Development Goals. Finding a couple of related SDGs for the project will 
allow the project‘s scope and goal to be aligned with the SDGs. This will enable people 
involved in the project to create results that benefit the planet and all those living on it.
Public transport is an important part of trying to create a sustainable world. It is linked 
to some of the UN‘s Sustainable Development Goals. Specifically, SDGs 9, 10 and 11 are 
closely related to public transport, and trains in particular. By setting goals for the project 
based on these SDGs and making sure the project scope matches, we can get a detailed 
and close look at possible solutions. This will also be useful for evaluating the results 
when the project is finished. This means that the 17 goals are describing specific targets, 
of which there are 169 in total. As well as 231 things that can be measured.
FIGURE 38: STG Overview
==PAGE:54==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
053
3.8.1 INDUSTRY INNOVATION AND INFRASTRUCTURE
RELATION TO THE PROJECT
The S-Train is one of the most important parts of Copenhagen‘s transport infrastructure. It plays a key role in meeting the needs of the city‘s residents and the demand for public transport. 
As a growing number of people move into the urban area and the S-Train system is reaching capacity, modernisation and upgrades are needed. The improvements include designing 
trains and stations to be equally accessible for everyone, improving the systems sustainability, and offering an affordable way to travel for all of the city‘s citizens. The S-Train is crucial 
for Copenhagen‘s economy, as many people rely on it for their commutes to work. By using renewable energy and fully electrified trains, it offers a sustainable way of travelling in the city. 
DSB plans to switch to driverless trains by 2035, which will make it the first large-scale urban train system in the world to be fully autonomous. This is a big step for innovation in the public 
transport sector. The overall goal is to create public transport infrastructure that encourages economic growth by being reliable and easy to use.
Industry, Innovation, and Infrastructure places emphasis on the establishment of resilient infrastructure, the promotion of inclusive and sustainable 
industrialisation, and the facilitation of innovation. [54]
RELEVANT TARGETS:
9.1 	 	
Develop quality, reliable, sustainable and resilient infrastructure, including regional and transborder infrastructure, to support economic 	 	
	
	
development and human well-being, with a focus on affordable and equitable access for all.
9.4		
By 2030, upgrade infrastructure and retrofit industries to make them sustainable, with increased resource-use efficiency and greater 		
	
	
	
adoption of clean and environmentally sound technologies and industrial processes, with all countries taking action in accordance with 	
	
	
	
their respective capabilities.

3.8.2 REDUCED INEQUALITIES
RELATION TO THE PROJECT
Many passengers face challenges when using the S-Train. Redesigning stations to make them more accessible to all would reduce inequalities in Copenhagen‘s public transport. Many 
people with disabilities depend on public transport to get around the city, which makes this very important. This includes making the stations easier to access for people who have for 
example difficulty moving around and blind people. It is also important to have affordable ticket prices that can be afforded by everyone, and to provide regular train services to all of the 
city‘s areas. This will allow citizens to move around the city reliably.
Reduced Inequalities aims to reduce inequality within and among countries by addressing differences in income, opportunities, and access to 
resources, making sure nobody is left behind. [55]

3.8.3 SUSTAINABLE CITIES AND COMMUNITIES
RELATION TO THE PROJECT
Copenhagen as a city often promotes its sustainable approach and lifestyle. Denmark as a country invests a lot of money in sustainability and many companies with this focus have also 
settled in the region. As a fully electric system, Copenhagen‘s S-Train is an important step towards social and environmental sustainability. It offers a way for all people to travel the city 
in an environmentally friendly and cost-effective way. Investments and upgrades in this area are therefore extremely important for the city to achieve its goal of being CO2-free by 2035 
and to maintain its sustainable image. As increasing numbers of people move to the urban area around Copenhagen, it is essential that the capital region is well connected. It is particu-
larly important to ensure that public transport is accessible to the largest possible number of people.
Sustainable Cities and Communities focuses on making cities and human settlements inclusive, safe, resilient, and sustainable. [56]

PROBLEM DEFINITION
NÆSTE STOP
This phase provides the methodological foundation for the later stages of the design process. Based on the research findings, 
the area of focus is precisely defined and a revised problem statement is created. This problem statement is based on the first 
definition of the task and the related research. Based on this, a narrowed down, concrete and fully defined problem statement is 
defined, which sets the direction for the solution development process. To ensure that the key challenges in terms of passenger 
experience are met, the problem statement is linked to relevant design principles. These focus on understanding the needs of 
passengers to enable intuitive and accessible use of the autonomous S-Train in the design solution. This summarised analysis 
of the challenge and the structured approach for the further process form the basis for the solution that is developed later. This 
solution is designed to consider and solve the real needs of the passengers through the problems and approaches defined in this 
step.
==PAGE:58==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
057
4.1 MAIN RESULTS
The insights gained from the research provide a solid basis for the next steps in the design process. They not only highlight key challenges in the project for Copenhagen‘s future 
autonomous S-Train system, but also provide direction for particularly important aspects of station-passenger interaction.
 
It is clear that a purely technical change to autonomous trains, with technical regulations and a safety system, will require further changes to the system. In order to achieve the desired 
efficiency and punctuality of the system, there are changes to be made in the areas of station design, information transfer and passenger guidance. Three main points have been identified 
that are of great importance for the project success.
EFFECTING PASSENGER EXPERIENCE THROUGH 
MISSING GUIDANCE AND INCLUSIVITY
A detailed analysis of the station and passengers has 
shown that many passengers face challenges using the 
S-Train due to their physical limitations. These problems 
lead to delays in service and affect the passenger. The 
overall travel experience can be severely compromised, 
which in the long run can lead to frustration and the 
avoidance of the S-Train. Existing assistance and 
information services often fulfil their function, but can 
be improved to be more inclusive and intuitive. These 
aspects are particularly important with the move to 
autonomous trains, as direct interaction with staff is 
no longer necessary and higher train frequencies leave 
less room for uncertainty. There is great potential in 
user-centred station design to address these issues 
and create an intuitive and accessible environment.
AUTOMATION FUNDAMENTALLY CHANGES THE WAY 
PASSENGERS INTERACT WITH THE SYSTEM
The change to driverless trains will fundamentally 
change the way the system and passengers interact. 
By taking out the train driver, a key part of the service 
that has often helped guide passengers. The driver also 
has to be in charge of stopping the train and closing 
the doors now, which is important for passengers with 
special needs who need extra help during their journey. 
In the new system, these passengers need to be 
catered for through more technical, visual, acoustic or 
personal assistance. To make this a reality, new design 
solutions will need to be created to help people accept 
an independent S-Train system. These designs will need 
to be created in the areas of operation, user-related and 
appearance.
EXISTING SOLUTIONS IN PUBLIC TRANSPORT ARE 
DIFFICULT TO IMPLEMENT
Due to the diversity of stations and the need to integrate 
into an existing system, many established systems used 
worldwide in public transport for safety and information 
are difficult to integrate into the Copenhagen S-Train 
system. The wide variety of locations and their 
requirements makes it difficult to develop a general 
solution that is applicable throughout. As a result, 
customised solutions often have to be developed to suit 
local conditions, which involves a great deal of effort 
and investment. Major modifications are often required, 
especially for passengers with some form of disability.
==PAGE:59==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
058
4.2 SOLUTION SPACE
The automation project being done by DSB for the Copenhagen S-Train can be split into 
four main problem and solution spaces. 
The first area is the automation itself, which deals with all the systems that need to be put 
in place to allow the trains to run reliably and safely. This includes signalling and safety 
systems. There are a many regulations surrounding this, and large teams are working on 
implementing these systems. The second area is the rolling stock itself, which needs to 
be either retrofitted or newly developed to handle the switch for driverless operation. 
DSB Also has dedicated teams for this. The third area is the station. As existing station 
infrastructure will be used, it is necessary to implement all the necessary changes and 
safety infrastructure onto existing platforms. Lastly, the passengers must be considered. 
It is important to understand the needs and behaviours of passengers to create an 
accessible and reliable system. All these areas overlap; while the station needs to be 
able to handle the train and the necessary systems for driverless operations, it must 
also meet passenger needs.
The main focus of this project is on the area between the station and the passengers, 
because there is a lack of focus and resources in the approach of DSB towards this change 
to driverless operations. There are numerous opportunities to improve efficiency and 
accessibility in this area, focusing on enhancements that go beyond essential safety and 
operational systems. By addressing softer, less-defined challenges, the aim is to create a 
more accessible and seamless experience. This involves redesigning parts of the station 
to provide better passage and an improved user experience, leveraging the transition to 
autonomous trains as a catalyst for these changes. This has to be done by also taking 
into consideration the requirements, the automation system and trains dictate.

PASSENGER

4.3 DESIGN PRINCIPALS
Especially in complex systems such as public transport, where the interaction between 
product and user is so regular but also so variable, a structural design process plays an 
important role. This is the only way to make decisions that are both user-friendly and 
functional. In the context of the automation of the Copenhagen S-Train, it is particularly 
important to consider issues such as problem solving, decision making and passenger 
navigation, which must now be carried out independently due to the removal of the driver. 
To ensure this, and to make the system accessible and understandable to all users, 
it helps to integrate design principles into the process. Public transport as a complex 
system, with its various different contexts and requirements, provides a large design 
field. For example, where do passengers who use the S-Train every day to commute 
prioritise punctuality while tourists want clear guidance through clear information and 
signposting. This diverse field of requirements and possibilities can be systematically 
analysed and eventually addressed through the use of design principles.
These principles are essential tools for recognising user needs, identifying problems 
and developing solutions, always linking the individual steps together. They aim not only 
at the aesthetics and functionality of the solution, but also at a holistic approach that 
takes into account all aspects of a positive user experience. This approach considers 
all parts of travelling. This makes journeys easier and more enjoyable. It also makes sure 
that using the system is easy and you can predict what will happen. This is particularly 
important in self-driving systems where humans are not directly involved. 
Through the collaboration with Skylab, Universal Design is the main design principle 
in this project. [57] As the brief was already formulated in the direction of finding 
solutions that recognise user needs and thereby create inclusive solutions, this design 
principle runs through the entire work. Through Skylab and the DTU, many tools are 
already available to shape a project with a universal design approach at all stages. The 
aim is to develop a solution that is as user-centred as possible, while leaving no user 
behind. Another essential tool is Design Thinking, which uses a problem-oriented and 
iterative approach. In this way, the proposed solutions are constantly evaluated and 
can be improved or steered in a better direction by repeating some steps during the 
process.
In addition, there are many other design principles that can be applied. These principles 
are often combined or mixed as many concepts and approaches, especially those 
focused on usability-oriented design, are quite similar. For example, co-creation, human-
centred design and user experience design all emphasise collaboration with users and a 
commitment to their needs.
The systematic application of these design principles creates a holistic design strategy 
that evolves by complementing the principles to create the best product for the user. 
It is very helpful that these principles provide approaches that go beyond theoretical 
concepts and aim at the practical application of solutions that promote inclusivity and 
usability. This ensures that the solution developed does not ignore important user 
groups.
==PAGE:61==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
060
4.3.1 UNIVERSAL DESIGN
As a design principle, universal design is based on designing products, services and 
environments so that as many people as possible can use them. The aim is to include 
people regardless of age, disability or other individual needs. Rather than removing 
barriers after implementation, the principle of universal design is to build in barriers from 
the outset to accommodate people with a wide range of needs. [58] A particular approach 
of Universal Design is not only to ensure that as many people as possible benefit from 
the products or solutions developed in projects, but also to focus on inclusivity within 
the project and team. This approach should ensure that inclusivity and accessibility are 
not seen as just one aspect of a long list of requirements, but are included in the work 
process at every step of the project. This is essential for achieving truly helpful results 
for those affected.
In the field of public transport, universal design, plays a particularly important role 
as accessibility for everyone should not be an afterthought, but in integral part of 
the design and development process. This requires the creation of a consistent and 
effective infrastructure that is accessible to all, including people with impairments. 
Particular attention must be paid to a well-structured environment, especially when 
changing between different modes of transport or when boarding and exiting stations 
and trains. Many people with disabilities rely on public transport to get around the city 
and participate in social life. If this is not the case, blind people, for example, who cannot 
drive themselves, may be restricted in their freedom of movement and thus excluded 
from public life and from things that many people take for granted.
By effectively applying a universal design approach, it is possible to create an 
environment that is not only more inclusive for people with disabilities, but also benefits 
all travellers. A station designed from the outset to be accessible and inclusive is safer, 
more comfortable and more usable for all travellers. For example, good infrastructure for 
people in wheelchairs, such as ramps and lifts, will also benefit mothers with strollers, 
travellers with heavy luggage or passengers with bicycles.
Focusing on the Copenhagen S-train and the driverless train project, a universal design 
approach can help to highlight the issues and challenges faced by passengers and 
ensure that they are addressed by all stakeholders throughout the project. This is the 
only way to achieve a sustainable and inclusive transition to an autonomous S-Train. It 
is therefore important that DSB is aware of this challenge and has the right tools and 
opportunities to work on these issues. Done correctly, this will not only bring social 
benefits to DSB through successful embedding, but the company can also benefit 
economically. Increased passenger satisfaction and system usability will reach a wider 
audience and therefore generate more revenue. This will create a long-term, efficient 
and profitable infrastructure for DSB.
At DTU, universal design plays a particularly important role in the Design and Innovation 
programme. Many projects aim to create products, systems and environments for 
everyone. For this purpose, DTU Skylab has developed the Universal Design Guide, which 
provides tools and approaches for projects to incorporate universal design. It is based 
on the seven design principles that can be used to create, evaluate and reflect.
•	Equitable Use
•	Flexibility In Use
•	Simple and Intuitive Use
•	Perceptible Information
•	Tolerance for Error
•	Low Physical Effort
•	Size and space for approach and Use
==PAGE:62==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
061
4.3.2 ABILITIES
The main tool that DTU Skylab has developed for the Technology Leaving No One Behind programme is the „Inclusion Prom Cards“. [59] This set of cards is designed to provide a deeper 
insight into the understanding of people‘s abilities and the fundamental principles of inclusive design. The card set contains two separate categories of cards. Firstly, the Ability Prom 
Cards, which are designed to identify the abilities related to the project and design proposal. They are also designed to assess the user‘s abilities and how this may affect their interaction 
with the design. To show that not only severe and permanent disabilities, which are often the first that come to mind, are relevant, they showcase three examples of disability for each 
ability in three categories.
PERMANENT
Permanent disabilities affect the user all the time. An example of this would be a wheelchair 
user who takes the S-Train and is affected by the limited mobility in all situations.
TEMPORARY
Temporary disabilities can affect the user over a period of time or if they occur regularly. 
An example of this is a person with an arm in a cast, but also in this project the passenger‘s 
movement is affected by regularly carrying the bike to the station.
SITUATIONAL
Situational disabilities only affect the user in a specific situation or for a short period of 
time. An example of this at the S-Train station could be rush hour traffic making it difficult 
to navigate the platform.
The goal is to showcase that even small abilities have a big effect on the solutions for 
some disabilities may affect a lot of areas, but also to show that solutions for small 
problems can benefit a large group of passengers. From the pool of activities that are 
most important and relevant to passengers on the train and in the stations, ten activities 
have been selected for this project. Work will continue with these ten activities in focus 
over the course of the project and the solution.

4.3.3 PRINCIPLES
The second type of cards included in the set are the principle prompt cards. [60] These cards detail principles that can be used in the design process to evaluate and reflect on solutions, 
concepts and ideas. The aim is to apply these principles to each step and to find out if a user group has been unconsciously forgotten in the previous process. If this is the case, the 
principles can be used together with the ability cards to highlight the forgotten users and their problems, so that the needs of this group are taken into account in the next iteration of 
the project and no one is forgotten.The great advantage of this is that they can be applied at any point in the project and are very helpful, for example, in the design thinking method. The 
principles are divided into three levels.
PRODUCTS/SERVICES
Principles at this level are aimed at applying universal design at a tangible level. They 
provide guidance for products and services.
ORGANISATION
Principles at this level are aimed to be applied at a higher level in order to integrate 
Universal Design into organisational practices and policies.
SOCIETY
Principles at this level are aimed even higher. The approach is to apply Universal Design 
at a societal level, in relation to the environment and surroundings.
The use of these principles makes it possible to directly test the approaches developed 
in the project and to evaluate whether all user groups are addressed. As the focus is 
on the passenger and their ability and acceptance to operate the solution, especially 
in public transport projects, there are some principles that are useful to apply to the 
project. Mostly they relate to the product and service level, which deals with how the user 
interacts with the developed solutions, but also on a social level it is important to include 
each individual in the project. In the future, it can be very helpful for DSB to incorporate 
these principles into the design process within their organisation.
Is the developed 
solution designed 
in such a way that it 
can be used by any 
user?
Is the developed 
solution designed in 
such a way that it can 
be utilised despite 
incorrect use by the 
user?
Is the developed 
solution designed 
in such a way that 
the user can easily 
understand it without 
prior knowledge?
Is the developed 
solution designed 
in such a way 
that it does not 
categorically exclude 
any users?

4.3.4 DESIGN THINKING
The Design Thinking method is based on solving problems through a user-centred 
approach. It puts people and their needs at the centre. The approach involves first fully 
understanding the problem, and then finding the best possible solution through creative 
and iterative action. Design thinking has an impact not only on the final solution, but also 
on the process itself. Inclusivity and adaptability are not just isolated requirements in 
the project plan, but are considered throughout all phases of the project from a variety 
of perspectives from the start of the project. The aim is not a one-size-fits-all solution 
fixed from the start, but one that is shaped by flexibility and adaptability throughout the 
process. [61]
The approach is based on a six-step process. This process is divided into three main 
stages. The first, Understand, is primarily concerned with the user and the problems they 
experience. At the end of this phase, a concrete problem is defined. The second phase, 
Explore, deals with possible solutions and ends with some kind of prototype. The final 
phase is Materialize. In this phase, the prototype is tested and then implemented. The 
UNDERSTAND
EXPLORE
MATERIALIZE
UNDERSTAND
DEFINE
IDEATE
PROTOTYPE
TEST
IMPLEMENT
1
2
3
4
5
6
design thinking approach consists in the possibility to go back during the process if the 
solution does not correspond to the desired result. This offers the advantage of being 
able to revise and change the findings and solution approaches over and over again, 
without losing the data collected.
The design thinking approach is suitable for the project involving the redesign of S-Train 
stations, as it places the passenger in the centre of attention. The aim is to find and solve 
real needs and not simply to act in accordance with technical standards and regulations. 
The aim is to create truly user-friendly spaces that benefit everyone. The first part of the 
design thinking method is particularly helpful in finding, analysing and understanding 
challenges and problems of passengers. In this way, a more inclusive and user-specific 
customised solution can be developed. Design thinking also emphasises that the solution 
is not only approached in isolation by one discipline or specialist area, but that solutions 
are created through interdisciplinary collaboration. By awakening empathy for the users, 
it is possible to make the entire team aware of challenges. This can be achieved best 
through direct contact with the people affected, for example through observation or

4.3.5 ADDITIONAL
Through the research and overall direction of the project, it has become clear that the relationship between the passenger and the S-Train system, be it the train or the station, is extremely 
important to the success of the project. Therefore, a user-, and human-centred design approach is essential to provide an intuitive and accessible travel experience. In addition to the 
two main design principles, there are other design methods that can be used to achieve a user-friendly design solution. These allow the passenger experience to be analysed in a holistic 
and user-centred manner and solutions to be designed accordingly. These additional design principles focus in particular on the interaction between the users, but also on a level in the 
project where all stakeholders have the same goals. They also consider the needs and interaction of the passenger in relation to the solution approach.
CO-CREATION
Co-creation as a design principle is based on the active 
involvement of each stakeholder in the design process. 
The main aim is to clearly communicate and define each 
individual‘s expectations and requirements through 
direct involvement. This ensures that everyone‘s needs 
are met by the final solution from the outset. [62] 
 
There are many parties involved in the project to 
automate the S-Train and redesign the stations, first and 
foremost DSB, which works closely with Banedanmark, 
the manufacturers of the new trains, construction 
companies and government institutions such as the 
Ministry of Transport. The technical expertise of all these 
parties is crucial to the project. In addition, passengers 
and employees can be involved in the design process, 
resulting in a more human-centred and inclusive 
solution.
HUMAN-CENTRED DESIGN
Human-centred design puts the individual, with his 
or her needs, abilities and limitations, at the centre of 
the project. It starts with a deep understanding of the 
user‘s physical, social and mental requirements. The 
technical design first takes a back seat to defining all 
possible user expectations. Only then can the design 
be developed to meet these needs. [63] 
In the case of the S-Train project, it is the passengers, 
but also the staff, who are in demand. Experiences, 
emotions and behaviour when using the S-Train system 
are extremely important in order to ultimately develop 
solutions that are not only technically possible, but 
will also be used by people in reality. This includes 
accessibility and social design, such as the emergency 
and assistance counters.
UX DESIGN
User Experience Design puts the user experience first. 
It is about creating a pleasant, efficient and stress-
free solution for the user. It focuses on how the user 
experiences and interacts with the environment and 
the objects within it. This applies to both the physical 
and digital interfaces between product and user.[64] 
 
In the S-Train project, this means that solutions must 
be designed in such a way that they can be understood 
and operated by as many users without requiring a great 
deal of prior knowledge or effort. This applies to every 
aspect of the passenger‘s interaction on the platform. 
An example with a special focus on the passenger with 
a bicycle would be the clear indication of the waiting 
position to enable the fastest possible boarding.

MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS

4.4 FINAL PROBLEM STATEMENT
PROBLEM STATEMENT: 
Passengers with bicycles need to be certain that they are waiting in the correct position on the platform to board the S-Train for their journey. 
How can these passengers be given better guidance to ensure they are waiting in the most appropriate position, there by improving operations and the passenger experience? 
Using the Copenhagen S-Train with a bicycle has practically become part of the city‘s 
culture. Many passengers use this method to get around the city. They cycle to the 
station, take the train for the greater part of the journey and then cycle the rest of the 
way to their final destination. Many commuters use this service, especially during rush 
hour. As the transport is free and any passenger can simply take their bike on the train, 
the practice has become well established by each age group. Today‘s trains are designed 
to accommodate many bicycles, with large bike compartments. However, the system is 
already reaching its limits, especially during rush hour. 
By switching to autonomous and driverless trains, DSB hopes to increase the frequency 
and efficiency of the services. This includes plans to reduce the time trains spend 
at stations. However, observations at stations have shown that the doors at which 
passengers with bicycles get on and off are often the last to close, thus allowing the 
train to continue its journey. This can jeopardises DSB‘s plan to reduce stopping times 
and therefore the effectiveness of the new system. In particular, the elimination of the 
driver makes such long delays less predictable. In order to counteract this as effectively 
as possible, it is extremely important that passengers travelling with bicycles wait at 
predetermined points so that they can board and disembark efficiently in the bicycle 
compartment. The current solution on the platform solves this problem with a bicycle 
painted on the platform, but this solution is very inflexible to changes in the operating 
schedule and unremarkable for passengers who rarely use this option. By switching to 
autonomous and driverless trains, DSB hopes to increase the frequency and efficiency 
of the trains. This includes plans to reduce the time trains spend at stations. However, 
observations at stations have shown that the doors where passengers with bicycles 
get on and off are often the last to close, allowing the train to continue its journey. This 
can jeopardize DSB‘s plan to reduce stopping times and therefore the effectiveness 
of the new system. In particular, the elimination of the driver makes such long delays 
less predictable. In order to counteract this as effectively as possible, it is extremely 
Based on all the research that has been carried out in the field of S-Bahn automation, it is now essential to define a precise problem to be addressed and solved by the solution being 
developed. It is important to formulate this as concretely and in as much detail as possible in order to provide a precise direction. This problem statement defines the further direction 
of the project and thus forms the centre of the design process. The aim is to define an area where research has shown that there exist problems that can be solved using a variety of 
approaches. However, the relevance to the passenger and the DSB must be given in order to find a relevant solution that will ultimately benefit the company and the passenger.

design process. The passenger using the S-Train with a bicycle has some added 
disabilities resulting from carrying the bicycle. If these limitations are taken into account 
in the development of the solution, not only the passenger with a bicycle will benefit, but 
also passengers with similar limitations.

SOLUTION
NÆSTE STOP
After a detailed analysis of the current situation, with a particular focus on passenger needs and challenges, the next step in the 
design process is to develop a solution based on these findings. The aim is to create a practical and applicable solution that focu-
ses on improving the passenger experience on public transport. 
 
While working on the solution, the focus of the project has evolved from a single, specific design solution to a methodical work-
shop format designed as a flexible tool for planners, designers and decision makers. The aim is to identify station and platform 
challenges and develop targeted solutions. In this chapter, the solution is characterised and applied once through the problem 
defined in the problem statement, thereby verifying it as a tool and creating a model solution based on the problem statement. 
The DSB can use this solution as an orientation for future projects in this direction.
==PAGE:70==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
069
5.1 FOCUS SHIFT
From the beginning of the project, the aim has been to ultimately develop a solution based 
on research that addresses the problem previously defined in the problem statement. 
This concrete design solution might have resulted in different types. The process was 
also approached in the same way to develop a concrete solution to the problem and to 
help passengers travelling with a bicycle to find the right place to wait on the platform. 
A number of possible approaches have already been identified in the research, such as 
the use of painted zones on the platform or screens to indicate the waiting areas.
From the research carried out, tools were developed at the beginning of the solution 
finding process, which made it possible and easy to analyse and represent passengers, 
their abilities and limitations, their behaviour and the relationships that result from this. 
This was used to create a type of matrix to create specific passenger types and evaluate 
their effects on the S-Train journey. Throughout the project, several approaches and 
tools have been developed in order to understand the needs and behaviour of the users. 
As a result, the direction of the project developed more and more towards an analysis tool 
for passenger behaviour. As these tools proved to be extremely flexible and informative, 
the project‘s focus shifted more towards a workshop that could use these tools and 
make the passengers‘ challenges and related problems visible. This workshop could 
then be used in public transport projects to precisely define and understand the needs 
and limitations of passengers in general and the desired target group. This change of 
focus means that the workshop itself will be designed and developed as a solution to 
the problem. The ultimate goal of the workshop is to quickly and effectively bundle the 
research and findings and make them usable for this and future projects. As a result, 
a concrete solution to the problem with a focus on passengers with bicycles took a 
secondary role.
However, this element has not been completely abandoned. It is this approach to the 
problem that has already been used in the tools developed before being combined into a 
workshop. As a result, there are already findings and approaches to the question of how 
best to guide passengers with bicycles. This is a situation where the problem statement 
can be used as an example to run through and test the functionality and effectiveness 
of the workshop. This will validate the approach and methodology of the workshop, but 
also provide solutions and results that can be used in relation to this problem should 
DSB want to develop solutions to this problem in the company in the future.
This change in approach also changes the perspective from which the problem is viewed. 
As the previous part of the work was already largely focused on the passenger and their 
needs, the outcome also changes in a more inclusive direction. The workshop should 
be designed to analyse a variety of problems, constraints and hurdles faced by a wide 
range of passengers. This provides a more holistic approach than focusing directly on 
the one group of passengers who use the S-Train by bicycle. It also creates two different 
sets of results. Firstly, the fully developed workshop, which can be applied to any public 
transport project. Those results are to be developed in such a way that they can be 
directly applied in various projects of DSB or DTU. And secondly, the solution developed by 
the workshop for passengers with bicycles, which shows possible solutions. In contrast 
to the workshop, these results only provide a rough direction and suggestions on how 
the problem could be tackled by DSB. Further work by DSB‘s engineers and employees is 
needed to create a fully functional and applicable solution.
==PAGE:71==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS

070
5.2 DIFFICULTIES OF A TARGETED APPROACH
The decision to shift the scope in this direction is based on a number of findings and 
circumstances. One of the most important and most striking is that the stations and 
infrastructure of the Copenhagen metro are very, very different and vary widely across 
the system. This is due to the fact that the system has been continuously expanded 
and modified over the past 90 years, which means that there are many different 
station designs with different requirements on the seven different lines. In contrast, 
the Copenhagen metro and the soon to be opened Letbane have been designed from 
the ground up and all the stations in one system are constructed in a similar manner. 
For the S-Train, this means that the introduction and implementation of new systems 
often require major changes, which involve significant investment. This makes it a 
challenging task to develop a standardised and efficient solution for the entire system.
This challenge was also clearly understood by the DSB in the run-up to the project, 
so the expectations regarding the project were focused on the research part. Due to 
the DSB‘s orientation in the future S-Train network and the distribution of resources, 
it is clear that the emphasis lies mainly on the safety and technical systems. This 
highlights the fact that the realistic introduction of a developed solution sspecific for 
the stations is highly unrealistic. It is possible to overcome this critical point by changing 
the focus and the plan to implement the results achieved throughout a workshop. 
This provides DSB with a fully developed product that can be adopted into the project 
immediately. In addition, it offers the opportunity for flexibility of use, providing solutions 
to more than just one problem.
This approach allows the research to be incorporated in a more long-lasting and effective 
form than an immediately focused solution, which could potentially get lost in the many 
requirements and challenges of the whole automation project. The methodological tool 
of the workshop can be introduced quickly and directly into the project without major 
changes, thus creating awareness for the challenges faced by passengers.
FIGURE 48: Modern and rural Farvhol Station
FIGURE 49: Old and central Dybbølsbro Station

071
5.3 WORKSHOP
The development and introduction of a workshop as a new primary solution aims to raise 
awareness of challenges in public spaces and to make employees and decision-makers 
involved in such projects aware of the versatility and impact they have on passengers.
In such large projects, with many key stakeholders, long timeframes and numerous 
regulations, it is often the case today that the needs of passengers, especially in the 
area of accessibility and inclusion of passengers with disabilities, are treated as one 
of many sub-aspects on the agenda of the design process. As a result, the impacts of 
theis field are often forgotten when working on the solution. This is also the case at DSB, 
where the division into different project teams and departments results in a tendency 
to underestimate the impact of individual work on the overall picture. It would therefore 
be extremely helpful to create a tool that clearly and visually shows people who are less 
involved in the passenger space how passengers interact with their solutions and how 
this interaction can ultimately be improved.
Compared to a single solution focussed on one problem, the workshop with its flexible 
and adaptable size provides a platform that can be applied to a variety of challenges and 
problems in different phases of the project. This versatility is particularly useful in the 
context of complex infrastructure projects, as regulations and passenger expectations 
change over time. A targeted solution that may seem innovative and modern now could be 
outdated in a few years‘ time. However, the workshop itself provides a tool that can react 
timelessly to the problems and can therefore be continuously improved and changed 
to incorporate new insights, stakeholder inputs or project goals. In this context, the 
workshop also has the advantage of achieving rapid collaboration between the individual 
stakeholders. These stakeholders, with their different backgrounds and experiences, 
can be brought together through a targeted workshop that is relatively short in time. 
Inclusive and holistic thinking can be incorporated into their work for the project. In this 
way, the participants not only learn the other persons‘ perspectives, but also develop 
a shared understanding of the solution and the role that each individual department 
plays in it. For the DSB, this has the advantage that the individual departments not only 
interact with each other when necessary, but also move away from isolated decision-
making. The fact that the workshop is designed in such a way that it is not seen as a dull 
meeting, but rather introduces a playful approach with hands-on elements, which is often 
lost in the everyday corporate life of many companies, plays a major role. By simulating 
the users, experience and journey in a playful scenario, participants in the workshop 
can develop empathy for how these users feel in the situation being worked on. This 
strengthens the sense of importance of these problems beyond a pure data analysis 
and provides an inclusive and user-friendly but design perspective. All of this means that 
the workshop can be used as an effective communication tool for the company. Due to 
technical expertise, it can sometimes be difficult to communicate ideas and approaches 
to other teams. Through the workshop it is possible to connect passengers, engineers, 
designers, planners, managers and others involved in the project without putting too 
much focus on one specific topic.
This approach changes the direction of the project in such a way that the research 
results obtained are not just applied to a specific problem and then become neglected. 
Instead, a long-term, usable solution is created that can also be used for projects far in 
the future. Developing and finalising the workshop as a dedicated product also provides 
the incentive to try it out, as it exists as a physical item and can therefore be added to 
the company‘s inventory.
==PAGE:73==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
072
5.3.1 WORKSHOP REQUIREMNETS
In order to create a useful tool in the workshop, it is important to establish some basic 
principles and outlines that relate to the real needs of DSB. This is the prerequisite for 
the workshop to be truly used later on, when it can be integrated into the actual daily 
routine of the employees.
TIMEFRAME
1 1/2 TO 2 HOURS
In close cooperation with DSB, a time frame of one and a half to two hours has been set 
for the standard workshop. This is based on experience in the company and the often 
busy schedules of the target participants. As this time frame is relatively short to cover 
all possible tasks and insights at once, the workshop is designed to adapt to the specific 
situation with varying objectives and focus, using different exercises to achieve the 
desired result. This creates a dynamic tool that can be used effectively at short notice. 
It also meets the purpose to showcase problem with a wide audience. This makes it 
possible to effectively integrate the workshop into the realistic day-to-day work of DSB 
staff, so that meaningful results can be achieved. Due to its relatively short duration, 
the workshop can also be used several times and spontaneously in different phases of 
the project with different participants. Due to the dynamic structure, longer workshop 
sessions can also be held if required.
PARTICIPANTS
2-10 PARTICIPANTS + WORKSHOP-LEADER
The workshop is designed in such a way that it can be carried out in a group of up to 
ten participants. As the target group of the workshop is based on the expertise in the 
company, it is geared towards people with background knowledge of railway infrastructure. 
This means that prior knowledge is required for some tasks. The composition of the 
groups can vary depending on the objectives and mission of the workshop. For example, 
groups of people from different departments and with different expertise can take 
part in a workshop. There can also be workshop set-ups in which only one department 
works specifically on a problem. It is also possible to include affected passengers in the 
workshop. The composition of the groups therefore always depends on the desired goal 
and focus of the workshop. A workshop is always led by a leader who defines the objective 
and structure in advance. To ensure an effective process, this workshop leader should 
be familiar with the workshop tool and have an overview of the problem to be worked on 
and the type of solution to be developed.
ORIENTATION
The workshop is designed as a tool for railway staff, especially DSB, to present problems, 
challenges and potentials at the station in interaction with the passenger. It should be 
used as a flexible tool for design and innovation projects. The main objective is to raise 
awareness of these problems, especially among passengers with disabilities, and thus 
form the basis for an inclusive design, with this target group in mind, who already have 
basic knowledge in this area. It should also be able to be used as a complementary asset 
in the design process at different stages of the project.
The objectives, timeframe and group size were developed in close co-operation with 
the DSB design team in order to be effectively embedded into the regular working day. 
This is crucial in order to be able to utilise the effectiveness by actually integrating the 
workshop into the projects.
==PAGE:74==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS

073
5.3.2 WORKSHOP SET-UP
ALL ABOARD!  INCLUSIVITY WORKSHOP
WORKSHOP SET-UP
DESCRIPTION: 
In order to run an organised and effective workshop, it is important for the workshop leader to establish a 
structure for the workshop before it starts. There are fixed points that must always be used. In between, 
the workshop leader is free to choose tasks from the pool of the All Aboard! Workshop and the Skylab 
Universal Design Guide to achieve the best possible results for the task. The workshop lasts one and a 
half to two hours and can be set up for 2-10 participants plus the workshop leader. 
INTRODUCTION
EXERCISES
CLOSING
5 MIN
100 MIN
5 MIN
Welcomes the workshop participants and thanks them for their 
participation. Gives a rough insight into the subjects to be dealt 
with and a schedule.
Firstly, a specific problem must be defined, which will 
be addressed in the workshop. This can be defined by 
the workshop leader or by all workshop participants in a 
brainstorming session. It is important to define a concrete 
scope in order to achieve relevant results in the short time 
available.
Depending on the duration and general objective of the 
workshop, the workshop leader can fill this period with 
various activities. For this, the workshop leader can use the 
tools provided in the All Aboard! Workshop. If other topics or 
objectives are dealt with, activities can also be selected from 
the Universal Design Guide from Skylab. It is important to have 
a precise structure before the start of the workshop and to 
schedule enough time.
PROBLEM DEFINITION
10 MIN
Thanks the participants once again for participating in the 
workshop, repeats the results clearly and ends the workshop.
70-
PARTICIPANTS: 
2-10 + Workshop-Leader
TIMEFRAME: 
      90-120 min
Before the workshop begins, a programme must be defined by the workshop leader. This 
programme is specific to each specific problem and can utilise the various exercises in 
different combinations. In this way, the right workshop can be created for every situation 
and problem. This is necessary above all due to the short time available, but also to avoid 
wasting resources unnecessarily on tasks that do not really contribute to solving the 
problem being worked on.
SET-UP
At the beginning of each workshop, there is an introduction in which the participants 
are welcomed and the aim and purpose of the workshop is explained by the workshop 
leader. This serves as an introduction and brings the participating persons up to speed, 
and and ensures the mood that it is now starting. It is extremely important for each 
workshop to then define the problem precisely. This is necessary in order to be able to 
carry out the tasks consciously afterwards and not to get lost in endless possibilities 
or options. This definition should be as precise as possible and the participants should 
all be involved with this problem in their own way, have expertise in it or be introduced 
to it. The time after that can be used freely by the workshop leader to choose the most 
effective and useful tasks for solving the problem that is to be addressed. It is important 
to plan enough time so that the individual tasks can be explained and carried out without 
any rush. This allows the workshop leader to create individual processes that are tailored 
to the problem. Finally, all participants are thanked again in the workshop and the results 
of the workshop are summarised again briefly and concisely.
Altogether, the workshop offers many different possibilities for approaching problems 
and creating solutions. With the help of this worksheet, it is easier to create well-defined 
workshops and, most importantly, to stick to the time frame. After all, good results can 
only be achieved if there is a relaxed atmosphere and enough time for the individual tasks.
