5.3.3 WORKSHOP VARIANTS
STATION REDESIGN
EXERCISES:
•	USER CASE DISCOVERY
•	USER CASE CREATION
•	
•	STATION PLAY THROUGH
A real station is to be renovated with a specific problem in mind. The aim of the workshop 
is to understand how exactly this problem arises and how it could ultimately be solved. 
It starts with general scenarios that happen in the current station design and how they 
affect the design. Then a concrete user journey is created where the problem occurs. 
The morphology can be used to visualise the current station and prepare for the final 
step of playing it through. In this case, the current station layout is recreated with the 
individual elements and changes can be made dynamically to see how this affects the 
problem.
The great flexibility of the workshop allows it to be used in many different areas and at many different stages of the design process for inclusive stations. This provides several opportunities 
to address different problems and develop solutions through the workshop. In order to develop relevant solutions, different orientations can be planned with the workshop set-up and 
different processes and tasks can be applied. The workshop leader should therefore consider in advance which activities and process would be useful for the scope of the workshop. This 
is necessary because in a normal scenario there is not enough time to go through all the tasks. Also, not all tasks are always necessary to find the best possible solution. The workshop 
leader must therefore be clear about what needs to be worked on, and we will select the appropriate exercises.
The goal and problem therefore define how the workshop is structured and which of the exercises are used. Here are three examples:
INCLUSIVITY FOR WHEELCHAIR USER
EXERCISES:
•	PERSONA DISCOVERY
•	PERSONA CREATION
•	USER CASE CREATION
•	STATION PLAY THROUGH
In this workshop scenario, an inclusive station for passengers with wheelchairs should 
be developed. It will start with a general look at how different types of passengers move 
around the platform. This creates awareness of movement patterns. Then a concrete 
problem persona is developed, which represents the abilities and challenges of the 
wheelchair user. This will be used for further work in the course of the workshop. The 
concrete user case for this persona is then drawn up. Every step and interaction on the 
platform is depicted. The last step in the process of working through the problem is to 
play through the current situation once and then the various elements on the station 
can be changed to create a better user experience.
==PAGE:76==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
075
5.4 EXERCISES
PASSENGER WITH BIKE
EXERCISES:
•	PERSONA DISCOVERY
•	PERSONA CREATION
•	USER CASE CREATION
•	STATION PLAY THROUGH
The problem is that passengers with bicycles often do not know the exact position where 
they are supposed to wait. As an introduction to the workshop, all participants will play 
through the challenges that different people with bicycles can experience on an S-Train 
journey. A specific problem persona is then created for precisely this scenario. The next 
step is to create the user case of the previously created persona while taking the S-Train 
with a bicycle. This situation is then played out with the station station and changes to 
the information system for waiting areas or other new elements are modified and added.
The main part of the workshop consists of various exercises that can be arranged in 
different combinations. These exercises are designed to summarise the results of the 
research phase in a structured way, so that these results can be applied not only to a 
single problem, but to many different situations. The exercises are designed to help 
participants recognise and reflect on complex relationships between passenger needs, 
station design and user interaction.
The three main parts of the research are covered by the exercises. A major focus is on 
understanding the passenger and their abilities and requirements. This is necessary to 
define and understand the subsequent interaction. The second point is the passenger‘s 
behaviour on the platform. The previously defined abilities play a role here, but there 
are also other points that are particularly related to the interaction with elements on 
the station. The third part focuses on the station itself. In order to solve problems in 
relation to existing stations, the current situation must be recognised and analysed. All 
three topics overlap and thus form an image that covers all areas of an S-Bahn journey in 
relation to the station.
The central element of the workshop is a task in which all the previously defined relevant 
actions of an S-Train journey are combined. This is realised in a playful and hands-on way 
to achieve maximum interaction between the workshop participants and to generate 
dynamic and creative solution approaches. This exercise is modular in design so that it 
can be applied to many different problems and solutions. The workshop leader can also 
modify each exercise slightly to best suit the problem to be solved.
In relation to the defined problem statements and the focus on passengers with bicycles, 
a similar approach can be chosen as for passengers with wheelchairs. With this method, 
the passenger‘s interactions can be clearly defined and the elements that the passenger 
uses on the platform can be clearly stated. Whether these elements are considered 
helpful, neutral or disruptive always depends on the person created in the workshop. This 
approach makes it possible to ultimately find solutions that can transform the station in 
terms of creating better guidance for waiting areas to be used by these passengers. 
In this workshop too, the main part in which the most solutions are created is playing 
through with the station time. This is why the most time should also be planned for this. 
This structure of exercises was also chosen for the test run of the workshop, which deals 
with precisely this problem statement. (Appendix 7.6)
==PAGE:77==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS

5.4.1 PERSONA
The research has shown how important the individual abilites of the passengers are and 
how strongly they affect the journey experience. To emphasise this issue to the workshop 
participants, there are two tasks that deal with this.
1. PERSONA DISCOVERY
The aim of this task is to put the participant in the situation of using the S-Train with 
restrictions. For this purpose, ready-made examples of personas are handed out, all of 
which have different limitations. Now the participants put themselves in the position 
of this persona and consider the problem previously defined for the workshop from the 
perspective of this persona. This creates empathy for this scenario and makes it easier 
to recognise the problems that exactly these passengers may experience and to better 
define their impact.
2. PERSONA CREATION
This task is performed when a specific target group exists for the problem to be addressed 
in the workshop. This persona is then created from a blank persona by all participants in the 
workshop and with the help of this person‘s individual characteristics and preferences, 
the points on the journey where problems may occur can be specifically explained.
With regard to the question of how a more effective and better-managed waiting process 
is possible for passengers who use the S-Train with a bicycle, a specific persona was 
created, which is the target persona for this question. Based on the fact that the person 
with a bicycle is travelling, the characteristics and requirements of this person were 
defined, which produces a basis that can be worked with in the further stages of the 
workshop.

5.4.2 USER CASES
ALL ABOARD!  INCLUSIVITY WORKSHOP
PARTICIPANTS: 
2-10
TIME: 
 
 
30	min
TOOLS: 
 
 
User Cases
USER CASES
INTRODUCTION
EXERCISE
DISCUSSION
5 MIN
15 MIN
10 MIN
The	 task	 is	 presented	 and	 the	 user	 cases	 are	 handed	 out.	
Depending	 on	 the	 size	 of	 the	 workshop,	 groups	 are	 formed	 to	
share	the	user	cases.
During	the	fifteen	minutes,	the	participants	go	through	the	
user	case	assigned	to	them	and	try	to	put	themselves	in	this	
situation.	In	doing	so,	they	should	pay	attention	to	problems	and	
scenarios	that	arise.	At	the	same	time,	they	should	think	about	
ways	in	which	this	situation	or	difficulty	could	be	improved.	For	
example,	screens,	announcements	or	waiting	areas.
The	group	discusses	the	problems	and	solutions	identified	in	
the	task	for	the	various	user	cases.	Important	or	special	findings	
related	to	the	problem	are	highlighted	and	noted	down.
1. USER CASE DISCOVERY
DESCRIPTION: 
The	aim	of	this	exercise	is	to	understand	different	situations	and	
circumstances	that	regularly	happen	at	the	train	station	that	
affect	the	passengers	and	their	impact	is	analyzed.
INTRODUCTION
EXERCISE
DISCUSSION
5 MIN
10 MIN
5 MIN
The	task	is	introduced	and	the	user	case	worksheet	is	placed	in	
the	centre	of	the	group.
The	group	works	together	to	develop	a	passenger	journey	based	
on	the	defined	problem	of	the	workshop.	The	individual	steps	
that	the	passenger	goes	through	are	drawn	on	the	worksheet	
and	potential	solutions	are	noted.
The	scenarios	and	solutions	developed	are	discussed	again	
in	the	group	and	any	particular	difficulties	or	potentials	are	
highlighted.
2. USER CASE CREATION
PARTICIPANTS: 
2-10
TIME: 
 
 
20	min
TOOLS: 
 
 
User	Cases	Blank
DESCRIPTION: 
The	aim	of	this	task	is	to	develop	a	user	case	that	outlines	the	
problem	of	the	workshop.	Challenges	and	scenarios	experienced	
by	this	passenger	should	be	emphasised.
In conjunction with the persona, which represents the capabilities of the passengers, it 
is important to understand how these passengers behave in the station and what steps 
their journey includes. The interactions they have with which assets are analysed to 
highlight potential problem areas.
1. USER CASE DISCOVERY
In this phase, it is essential to show the participants what situations can happen at the 
station. For this purpose, example scenarios of user cases that happen regularly are 
handed out in this task. In connection with the defined workshop problem, the participants 
can analyse where this user case would change or experience challenges if the problem 
addressed were to occur. This is a good way of raising awareness of the importance of 
this problem.
2. USER CASE CREATION
Problems or solution approaches in DSB design projects are often related to user 
interaction with various assets on the platform. In order to precisely define this interaction 
and link it to the identified workshop problem, a persona-specific user journey is created 
for this problem in the course of this task. To do this, all the individual steps that these 
users take during their journey are analysed and written down. This creates a detailed 
picture of which existing station infrastructures are interacted with.
For the person using the S-Train by bike, it is important to understand how they will 
behave at the station. For this purpose, specific user cases are created that show each 
individual step. To get as accurate a picture as possible of this persona, it makes sense 
to present three different versions of the same situation in order to identify different 
effects of interaction. This involves depicting normal use of the S-Train with a bike, the 
situation when everything runs perfectly and when many problems occur.

FUTURE STATE
In this task, the workshop participants can analyse the structure of specific stations 
that are currently existing in the system. This is important for workshops dealing with 
problems related to specific stations. The basis for recognising the local conditions is 
established through these excersices.
1. STATION CURRENT STATE ANALYSIS
For many problems, it is necessary to analyse a real station in order to clearly present 
the local conditions and communicate with everyone. This exercise serves to provide 
any S-Bahn station in the Copenhagen S-Train and the associated layout. This way, the 
current situation can be visually and clearly presented to all participants.
2. STATION PERFECT STATE CREATION
To solve some problems, a major redesign of the station is necessary. This task can be 
used to determine what this redesign might look like and which elements are needed 
at the station to address the problem. Participants can choose from the given example 
elements or add elements that they think would be helpful for the solution based on their 
expertise. This results in a clear picture of the features needed for the desired station.
The station morphology is not essential for the problem of passengers travelling with 
bicycles and is not selected as a task in the workshop test run. This is the case because 
no specific station is dealt with and the assets located at the station that are relevant 
for this user case can also be presented and analysed in the next exercise.

step by step. This reveals the objects and circumstances with which this person interacts, allowing 
potential and problem areas to be identified. By using the modular and playful application method, the 
structure of the station can be changed at any time to optimise the passenger‘s interaction with the 
station and assets. This task can be combined with the other workshop tasks for more precise results.

FIGURE 58: Worksheet STATION PLAY THROUGH
All the previously identified and analysed facts, which were addressed by the workshop 
exercises, have to be put into context in the last step, as they are all interrelated. The 
persona with its abilities interacts with the elements present on the station through its 
user case. In order to present this complex interaction, this task is at the centre of each 
workshop. It involves playing through all the circumstances in the team in a playful way 
and creating them through dynamic interaction between the participating solutions.
STATION PLAY THROUGH
This exercise comes with a large tool kit to play through the situation and user journey at 
the station. A magnetic basis station forms the centre of this exercise. Various building 
blocks can be placed on it, which represent different aspects of elements on a station. 
This means that any station in the Copenhagen system can be configured and played 
through with a persona, as an example. In the next step, the structure of the station can 
be changed and the whole team can see how the passenger‘s prices and interaction 
with the elements at the station change. The big advantage of this system is that the 
modules make it very flexible and modular. The playful approach encourages the team to 
quickly play through different scenarios, visually and haptically perceiving the changes 
as they do so, which creates a great deal of awareness of the problem.
This exercise concludes the workshop for passengers travelling with bicycles as well. 
First, the normal case scenario at the station is played through, including the interaction 
between the individual elements such as information screens. After that, changing the 
position of the individual elements can create a better experience for the user, which is 
then recorded and serves as an important insight into what a station designed for these 
users might look like.

5.5 RESULTS 
By going through a workshop with the problem of designing an inclusive station to 
accommodate a passenger with a bicycle, not only was the workshop verified as a very 
effective tool, but solutions and insights were also gained to help us understand the 
user, their behaviour and possible solutions.
PERSONA
The person to whom the problem applies has been defined with all their abilities. It has 
been found that this person uses the S-Train regularly with a bicycle and has some 
difficulties and challenges. For example, the ability to press the door push buttons to enter 
the S-Train is often limited because the heavy bicycle has to be held with both hands. 
The person also often has problems when this particular heavy bike has to be carried up 
the stairs because the lift is broken again. Another point that this person experiences 
during their journey is that the train does not always stop at the station where the train 
is supposed to stop, and it is sometimes not possible to board the train directly at this 
station because the train is either longer or shorter and therefore the entrance is at a 
different position. Since the person wants a smooth transition between bicycle and train 
journey, it would be good to find a solution for this situation.
USER CASE
The person‘s path of action is always relatively similar. The same elements and information 
are used at the station. Sometimes, however, especially in the worst case, it is necessary 
to request more information or the same information again. In the worst case, this 
persona shows that a long route through the station is necessary to ultimately board the 
train. This long journey could have been avoided if more effective information had been 
provided, indicating where the bicycle compartment was located. The detailed analysis 
of these scenarios in connection with the person‘s abilities forms the basis for the next 
task.
STATION PLAY THROUGH
The station play-through produced some interesting results through the use of the 
workshop to varify and the workshop review. The previously defined user journeys were 
played through to visually illustrate the aspects described earlier. This often led to other 
situations or interactions that could be added to the user case or that the passenger 
would want to do. One important aspect that was noted was that the information about 
the location of the bicycle compartment varies with the length of the train and therefore 
with the stopping position of the train. This is a common situation, exacerbated by the 
fact that bicycles are painted on the floor and are often not visible to passengers. This 
leads to confusion and ultimately delays in operations. It was therefore decided that a 
clearer, more visible system was needed, one that would dynamically and visually indicate 
the stopping position of the bicycle compartment. This should enable the person with 
the bike to react to short-term or spontaneous changes and not hold up operations. 
Another point that was highlighted was that bicycles often block aisles if not placed 
on the platform in an organised manner, preventing effective passenger flow. This also 
means that people with bikes often have to hold them. A solution that would allow bikes 
to be kept in an organised manner and out of the way, while still allowing owners to have 
their hands free, could solve this problem.
The results of the workshop are the final deliverables of the workshop. These can then be 
taken up by DSB project teams and taken into account in the further development. In the 
case of the problem persona passenger with bicycle, possible solutions were discussed 
afterwards, which also serve as inspiration for DSB for future projects.
==PAGE:88==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
087
5.6 POSSIBLE SOLUTIONS 
SPOTLIGHT
The first solution is to install a spotlight system that projects a large, clearly visible circle 
of light with a bicycle symbol on the floor where the bicycle compartment is located. This 
will help to solve the problem of the varying stopping position of short and long trains 
and the associated change in the position of the bicycle compartment, which often 
leads to hectic situations and uncertainty among passengers. This helps passengers to 
know where to go and get on the train on time. Another benefit is that it also tells other 
passengers about the bicycle compartment, so they can use another door to board the 
train, when not travelling with a bike. The most important aspect of this solution is that it 
is flexible and can be changed as needed. The information about train arrivals and stops 
is already stored in the DSB system, so the Spotlight can react in real time to changes 
in the timetable or train length and unscheduled changes. This allows fast and efficient 
information transfer that can be seamlessly integrated into the existing infrastructure 
and information environment.
Working through and testing workshops led to various solutions for passengers with bicycles at the Copenhagen S-Train station. (Appendix 7.6) From this, potential solutions that 
emerged in the workshop can be recognised. These solutions are not finalised or fully developed design concepts, but rather an initial approach to the issue. On the one hand, these 
solutions offer inspiration for the future design of S-Train stations in relation to the full automation of the system for DSB. They also prove that the workshop approach was the useful way 
to go. The design approaches were developed based on the challenges experienced by passengers travelling by bicycle. Both proposed solutions demonstrate how specific challenges 
can be addressed through targeted passenger-related actions. They also show how the functional use of the platform and the passenger experience can be improved through simple but 
well thought-out design changes. Even if these approaches are only initial ideas, it is clear what potential can be tapped in order to guarantee operational processes in the autonomous 
system. These two results not only provide practical guidance for DSB for future infrastructure innovation, but also valuable insights into the effectiveness of the workshop. Still, there is 
a long way to go from these ideas to a realistic implementation, which will require a lot more time and expertise.
BIKE-STAND
This solution focusses on the comfort and safety of passengers with bicycles while waiting 
on the platform. The idea is to install a specially designed bicycle rack at the designated 
boarding areas for the bicycle compartments. These bike racks allow passengers to park 
their bikes safely while waiting, which both relieves physical strain and helps them to 
stay safely on the platform. Especially in busy stations or during peak travelling times, 
this enables a better distribution of people on the platform. It is also advantageous 
to give passengers the opportunity to carry out other necessary activities without a 
bicycle in their hands. The bike racks are designed to ensure quick and uncomplicated 
use. Passengers should be able to put their bikes in and take them out again in a few 
simple steps. At the same time, attention is paid to a space-saving design so as not to 
block valuable space on the platform. A clearly visible zone around this stand or a vibrant 
colour can also be selected to make the position of the stand clear from a distance. 
This not only creates practical functionality, but also the psychological advantage that 
passengers with bicycles are recognised as an important user group. This is particularly 
beneficial with the connection between passengers on the Copenhagen S-Trains and 
bicycles.

MASTERTHESIS

REFLECTION
NÆSTE STOP
The reflection phase of the project deals with the course of the project, reflecting and evaluating it. Consideration is given to 
the original objectives and the final outcome of the project. It shows what has been achieved and where there is still room for 
improvement. A summary is given of what exactly the project has delivered and developed for DSB and DTU, and how these 
results and tools can be used in the future. This chapter thus serves as a final evaluation of the project and shows how it has 
contributed to making Copenhagen‘s suburban railway system more inclusive in terms of future train automation.

Starting with research and analysis, this six-month design project followed a structured design process that led to a shift in focus from a single design solution to a workshop-based 
approach. Insights from observations, interviews and other means of research were translated into a practical tool for inclusive station design. The concluding step focused on testing, 
refinement and documentation to ensure real-world applicability.

090
6.2 CONCLUSION
The project‘s brief, formulated in collaboration with DSB and DTU, and the objectives 
derived from the research were successfully met. The aim of the project was to analyse 
the current situation and circumstances in order to develop a solution that would improve 
the passenger experience by redesigning the S-Train station in a way that was not 
necessarily dictated by specifications and regulations. The changeover of Copenhagen‘s 
S-Train system to autonomous driverless trains was used as an opportunity to implement 
this approach. As a result of this transition, the stations need to be optimised in terms of 
user-friendliness, guidance and accessibility for all passengers. 
A key outcome of this Master‘s thesis was the development of a workshop format that 
allows the challenges faced by different passenger groups to be made visible. The 
aim of this workshop is to serve as a tool to increase the awareness and attention of 
designers, engineers and decision makers in infrastructure projects, with a special 
focus on trains, in order to provide inclusive and user-friendly solutions, especially 
for passengers with disabilities. In order to maximise the effectiveness of the 
workshop and the problem defined in the mid-term presentation, the workshop was 
designed to address the issue of improving information given to passengers with 
bicycles. This allowed the identification of key problems for this group of passengers 
and the development of a variety of solutions. The workshop was also found to be 
an efficient way of identifying and discussing genuine problems of passengers. 
 
Based on extensive and user-oriented research, the workshop format allowed for 
a detailed analysis of actual user behaviour through observation, interviews and 
accompaniment of affected passengers. The findings were incorporated into the 
workshop in the most meaningful manner. As a result, the workshop format provides 
DSB and other stakeholders with a practical, flexible and long-term solution for 
integrating passenger-centred design into their own design process. Through 
its interactive nature, the workshop aims to address the needs of a wide range of 
user groups and enable inclusive solutions by raising awareness of these issues. 
 
The solutions developed during the workshop not only provide DSB with an example 
for the implementation of the workshop, but can also be used as a recommendation 
for future projects aimed at improving passenger guidance and interaction. 
 
The project shows that the consideration of human factors is crucial for the success of 
urban railway automation in the next decade. They not only play a role in the acceptance 
of the new system, but are also directly linked to its economic success. The inclusive 
design of public spaces and urban transport for all passengers will benefit not only those 
specifically targeted, but all users. The knowledge and methods gained will provide a 
sound basis for future development in the form of application and improvement of the 
workshop, and subsequently the practical implementation of the solution developed in 
the workshop in future infrastructure and projects.
The application and also the test of the workshop have shown that one of the greatest 
advantages of the tool is its great flexibility. It can represent any field and any situation that 
might occur at a railway station and thus provide a basis for improvement. For example, 
existing stations can be discussed, new stations can be planned or specific problems 
can be analysed. It is important that the workshop leader has an understanding of the 
workshop in order to develop relevant configurations and compositions that can deliver 
targeted results.
==PAGE:92==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
091
6.3 DISCUSSION
The workshop developed in the master‘s thesis provides DSB, but also DTU, with a useful 
and flexible tool to address problems of inclusion and accessibility in public transport. 
In the course of the project, the developed solution has evolved more and more in the 
direction of making the obstacles and challenges of passengers, especially those with 
disabilities, visible and integrating them into the design process.
At the beginning of the project, the focus was on a specific solution for passengers, 
especially with the design and innovation background of the study programme and the 
previous knowledge in product design, which ideally would also be implemented by the 
DSB in the stations. However, as the project progressed, it became clear that such a 
solution was likely to remain theoretical due to the wide variety of stations and budget 
constraints. However, the knowledge gained and the approaches taken could easily be 
converted into a tool that can be implemented in future DSB projects, leading to results 
that are actually realised. The tool can also be further developed in an academic context 
for use in projects with a similar theme. As a flexible tool, the workshop can also be 
modified in these projects to fulfill specific needs.. Although the solution developed for 
the problem defined in the mid-term presentation has been pushed to the background, 
it still provides interesting results and serves as a good illustration of how the workshop 
works. 
At the beginning of the project, DSB‘s expectations focused on a comprehensive study 
of passenger behaviour and the resulting constraints. How would this affect a transition 
to a driverless train system, and how could this behaviour be changed and passengers be 
guided in such a way that the system would also benefit from the new efficiency achieved 
from the passenger side. The emphasis was therefore never on the solution developed 
on behalf of the company. However, the workshop has now resulted in a solution that 
the DSB can also use for its own future projects. In the end, the expectations of both the 
university and the company could be combined and fulfilled. This has been made possible 
by an in-depth and particularly user-centred research approach. This has provided the 
project with a great deal of useful data and insight, and has shown how differently the 
challenges and environment can affect each individual passenger. It is also because of 
this diversity that the project has developed in the direction it has now taken.
The project would have benefited from a more detailed testing period with the actual 
target group for the workshop. Unfortunately, these tests were only included in the 
schedule after the project had been completed. It might also have been possible to 
make the workshop a little more extensive in order to get more of the findings across to 
the participants. However, this is not possible due to the busy schedules of DSB staff, 
which is why DSB limited the workshop to a maximum of 2 hours. A one-day workshop 
is probably a more effective and long-term solution in terms of raising awareness. The 
previously mentioned diversity of passenger challenges is also a factor that could have 
been improved. Although the project covers a wide range of passengers, it is still possible 
that groups and individuals have specific challenges that have not been identified and 
therefore become a problem when travelling on the S-Train.
Overall, the project delivers good results that can be applied by DSB. The main aim is 
to raise awareness and communicate to all staff involved in such large projects that 
passengers can have major problems using the products, resulting from even the most 
minor challenges. Addressing and solving these problems will ultimately benefit all 
passengers. Future DSB or DTU projects can build on these results. The tools can also 
be expanded, especially by DTU, as there are still many areas of public and social life that 
have not yet been designed inclusively.
==PAGE:93==
MASTERTHESIS
HENDRIK STEGERS
PAGE XXX
092
6.3.1 DTU PROCESS
Throughout the Design and Innovation programme, one of the main principles of how 
design projects are approached, both at Bachelor and Master level, is the double diamond 
model. This is applied both consciously and often unconsciously to design projects in 
order to understand and analyse the process of the project. The design project for an 
inclusive S-Train station discussed in this Master‘s thesis can also be examined in this 
way. 
At the beginning of the project, the focus was set on research to analyse the scope 
and possibilities, after which, in the middle of the project, a precise problem statement 
was defined, for which solutions were to be found in the second phase. The problem 
statement was presented in the mid-term presentation and then defined in more detail 
in cooperation with all stakeholders. The next step was to find and develop solutions. 
During this process, the focus shifted progressively to a workshop addressing the 
problem of an inclusive S-Train station for all passengers. As a result, the development 
of a concrete solution moved further into the background, and the workshop became 
part of the final product and thus a final step in the double diamond process. However, in 
order to demonstrate the possibilities and options of the workshop, work continued with 
the problem statements defined in the mid-term presentation. This problem statement 
was used to test the functionality and applicability of the workshop. At the end of the 
project, this resulted in two separate solutions. Firstly, the fully defined and elaborated 
workshop that can be used by DSB or other companies without further preparation. And 
secondly, the broad results that emerged in relation to the problem statement by working 
through the workshop as it developed.
For the DTU, the delivery of the thesis marks the end of the project for the time being. 
However, further development or use of the workshop is possible in the future.

6.3.2 DSB PROCESS
For the DSB, however, an additional diamond can now be added as an extra step in the 
project. So far, the project has only provided rough ideas and solutions, so the DSB can 
now use the workshop as a tool to develop concrete solutions to the problems they face. 
The problems defined in the master‘s thesis or other problems related to the station can 
be discussed with those who are involved in the subject. In this way, the DSB can use 
the workshop to tackle its own problems in the project of the future automation of the 
S-Train, but also in other projects of the company. 
The great advantage is that this process can be repeated over and over again. The third 
diamond is not tied to a specific topic, but can be used by DSBs in relation to their current 
projects. The workshop based on the research thus serves as a tool that the DSB can 
use flexibly. When a project needs to solve user-focused problems, the workshop can 
be used and the research can be integrated into the project in an efficient and simple 
way. In this way, useful and effective solutions can be created. These solutions are then 
defined by DSB and have a high degree of integration into the system, leading to real 
change through a more inclusive S-Train. If further customisation is required in the 
future, this will be possible, as will the flexible workshop, providing a highly future-proof 
tool that can be expanded and used by the DSB.

6.4 FUTURE WORK
The completion of the DTU master thesis project does not mean that the project for 
an inclusive S-Train station is finished. There will always be room for improvement to 
make public life, and especially public transport, more inclusive for people with all kinds 
of disabilities. However, this requires user-centred and targeted solutions. This project 
provides an important and useful tool for designing such projects in an inclusive way in 
the future.
As a result of the close collaboration with the DSB during the project, it has already 
been agreed that a workshop will be held in the coming months with staff involved in the 
future S-Train project. For the DSB, it is of utmost importance to give these participants, 
who deal intensively with technical regulations and data, an insight into how many small 
obstacles and difficulties there are for passengers with any kind of disability in the daily 
use of the S-train. Through the workshop and the empathy with different personas 
with disabilities and the subsequent play-through, the aim is to draw attention to these 
issues so that in the future course of the project, employees will automatically think 
of such circumstances and possible solutions. At the moment, only one workshop is 
planned to test the effectiveness of the workshop methods developed. If it proves to 
be a success, the project team will be able to use the tool in future situations when 
designing passenger-oriented parts. This does not necessarily have to be the future 
S-Train project, but can also be applied to all other offers and projects of the company. 
If, during the first run-through, it becomes apparent that there is room for improvement, 
these can be made in order to deliver the best possible tool for DSB. The ultimate goal is 
to introduce the workshop as an integral part of every project at DSB, thus establishing 
a permanent awareness of these issues in the company and design culture. Once this 
has been achieved, the neutral design of the workshop means that it can also be used by 
other companies and stakeholders in the public transport sector. The research that has 
been carried out and the solution that has been developed for passengers with bicycles 
are also of great value to DSB. Even if much of it is unlikely to be directly applicable due 
to cost and time constraints. They provide a good basis for future projects to tackle this 
very problem.
The workshop can also be of great benefit to DTU. It can be made available to students in 
addition to the tools already developed for the Universal Design Guide by the Universal 
Design team in Skylab. Many projects within the design and innovation cosmos are dealing 
with the design of public transport, but are often involved in shorter and less extensive 
projects. Through the workshop, other students can benefit from the research already 
done and use it for their projects. It is also possible to expand the pool of tools and 
exercises through further work on the current workshop. Currently, there is still a strong 
focus on trains. However, due to the similarity with other areas of design for people, it is 
an opportunity to create a version for inclusive and accessible urban design, as there is 
a lot of overlap there due to the users. In general, the workshop as a tool is very dynamic 
and can always be developed further. The DSB, DTU or others can adapt and develop the 
tool to suit their needs.
In all cases, the best option is still to talk to the people who have difficulties in public spaces 
and in using public transport and who are therefore excluded. With the tools developed, 
it is possible to make these problems clear to people and to visualise them much more 
vividly. This creates the opportunity to create a better public space for everyone.

APPENDIX
NÆSTE STOP
The appendix of this thesis provides a comprehensive collection of materials, methods and supplementary informa-
tion gathered during the design process. Content described in the main section is expanded upon and provides ad-
ditional context to clarify the comprehensibility of the approach. The research results, which were compiled through 
observation and interviews, are shown in detail. The extensive photo series, which was created in the course of the 
work, is also included in this part of the thesis. The results of the trial run of the workshop are also shown. 
 
This chapter thus concludes the project and provides all the relevant information that was compiled during the pro-
ject.

7.2 OBSERVATIONS
Observations were carried out to gain a more precise understanding of the current situation at the stations and on the platform. These observations were documented in detail in order 
to gain relevant insights.
STATION: 	 	
VESTERPORT
DATE:	 	
	
10.9.24
TIME:	
	
	
13:00 - 14:00
TYPE:	 	
	
ISLAND
TRACKS:	
	
2
LINES:
GENERAL OBSERVATIONS:
•	Most bikes on escalators
•	Different train lengths can be confusing, special parts of the train. (Bikes, baby 
strollers)
•	Only information screens on the entrances, no in the middle
•	Very high frequency of trains.
•	Unsure about effectiveness of signs on trains
•	Announcements made by trains not the platform
•	No announcements sometimes
•	Quiet announcements sometimes
•	Loud peeping sounds for doors
•	Beeping sometimes disturbs announcements
•	First tight spot creates kind of a barrier
•	Station name sings very close to one side of the platform
•	Person consumed drugs in waiting booth

7.2.4 OBSERVATION 4
STATION: 	 	
AMAGERBRO
DATE:	 	
	
17.9.24
TIME:	
	
	
13:30-14:00
TYPE:	 	
	
ISLAND
TRACKS:	
	
2
LINES:
GENERAL OBSERVATIONS: 
•	General observations
•	People tend to wait in the areas with metal railings and lines for doors.
•	The area at the end of the station is basically unused.
•	Clear and constant announcements for train directions
•	Queues can form at the lifts (bicycles, wheelchairs, prams)
•	Elevator at one end, next exit escalators, then enter escalators
•	People tend to enter without waiting, making queues less effective
•	Platform doors and walls provide security
•	Stop times are very short
•	There is always a metro staff member in the station.
•	Sound and light signal when doors close
•	Quite a few people leave the train after people have entered.
•	Possible queues at the exit escalators after a busy train
•	Information about broken lifts displayed on the screen
•	Displaying information to passengers about special events on the screens

7.3 PASSENGER ACCOMPANIMENT
In cooperation with the Skylab, passengers of the S-Train can be contacted who were willing to make a typical journey with the S-Train and be accompanied. By accompanying the 
passengers, problems, opportunities and other points were to be identified. The two profiles and the notes collected are described.
7.3.1 PROFILES
	
	
	
	
	
MATHILDE
	
	
	
	
	
NAME: 	 	
	
Mathilde Saltoft Schou 
	
	
	
	
	
AGE:	
	
	
23
	
	
	
	
	
Occupation:	
Student
	
	
	
	
	
Disability:	 	
Autism and ADHD
Matilde is a student, she studies at the DTU and uses the S-Train mainly for commuting 
to university or work. Due to her disability, she has a number of difficulties when travelling 
on the S-Train. The main problem is sensory overload. Quiet areas, waiting zones and 
clear information through apps, announcements and signage are important to her.
	
	
	
	
	
MICHAEL
	
	
	
	
	
NAME: 	 	
	
Michael Starhol 
	
	
	
	
	
AGE:	
	
	
36
	
	
	
	
	
Occupation:	
	
	
	
	
	
Disability:	 	
Blind
Michael is totally blind and mainly uses the S-Bahn to get to work. He travels from Valby 
to Glostrup and has to change trains at one point. As a blind person, he is particularly 
dependent on tactile infrastructure, announcements and audio signals. Over time he 
has developed a routine for his main route, but he has some difficulties with routes he 
doesn‘t know so well, and has to prepare for them.
FIGURE XX: Photo Mathilde
FIGURE XX: Photo Michael

•	Change at Carlsberg, because Valby he has to go up and down stairs

7.4 PHOTO RESEARCH
During the course of the project, photos were taken at various locations and in various fields that are relevant to the project. These could be about the structure and systems of railway 
stations, how passengers interact with them or how other sectors deal with the topic of crowd control. These photos give a broad overview of solutions and applications in the research 
field.

7.5 PASSENGER CONFIGURATER
This tool provides a detailed overview of the ten defined abilities that are relevant for passengers at the S-Train station. The configurator provides examples for each ability releted to 
each level and each magnitude. This makes it easy to recognise and define individual personas and behavior patterns. Relevant impact areas are also provided for orientation.

7.6 WORKSHOP TEST
A test run in which the individual tasks of the workshop are tested and the effectiveness of the method can be determined. The test was not carried out with DSB employees, but with DTU 
students. These students have experience with in design and were therefore able to evaluate the layout and structure as well as the individual tasks.
